10 min
First Face of the Philosophers' Proof for Time
Al-Jurjani presents the first face of the philosophers' proof for time's existence via motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' first proof for time's existence works by comparing motions of equal speed to infer an extended potentiality (imkan).

Core Idea
Time is an extended potentiality derived from comparing motions.

The philosophers, particularly Ibn Sina, advanced two proofs for the reality of time. The first proof uses the concept of 'imkan'—an extended potentiality that accommodates motion. By assuming two motions of equal speed and varying their start and end times, the philosophers argue that there must be an underlying extended thing that is not merely the distance or the motion itself. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial hypothesis
Start with the assumption of two motions of equal speed. Note how the author sets up the scenario of simultaneous start and end.
This establishes a baseline for comparing imkan.
2
Understand the concept of imkan
Imkan is an extended potentiality that accommodates the motion. Recognize that it is not the distance itself but something that allows the motion to occur.
The term is key to the proof.
3
Follow the variations
Consider cases where one motion starts earlier or ends later. Notice how the imkan changes, becoming a part of the original imkan.
This shows that imkan is divisible and extended.
4
Examine the commentator's refinements
Al-Jurjani discusses Ibn Sina's use of motions differing in speed to show imkan is not identical to distance, and addresses objections.
These refinements clarify the proof's logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالكلية احتج الحكماء على وجود الزمان بوجهين الأول أنا نفرض حركة في مسافة معينة على مقدار من السرعة ونفرض حركة أخرى مثلها في السرعة فإن ابتدأتا معاً وانقطعتا معاً قطعتا تلك المسافة المعينة معاً فبين ابتداء حركة السريع الأول وانتهائها إمكان أي أمر ممتد يسع قطع تلك المسافة المخصوصة بتلك السرعة المعينة ألا ترى أن السريع الثاني لما شاركه في ذلك الإمكان وتلك السرعة قطع أيضاً مقدار تلك المسافة ولو فرض ألف حركة على هذه الحالة وجب تساويها في مقدار المسافة ولا يجوز تفاوتها في ذلك أصلاً وإن ابتدأت إحداهما قبل أي قبل الأخرى وانقطعتا معاً أو انقطعت إحداهما قبل وابتدأتا معاً قطعت الحركة المتأخرة في الابتداء على التقدير الأول، والحركة المتقدمة في الانقطاع على التقدير الثاني مسافة أقل من مسافة صاحبتها فبين ابتداء الحركة المتأخرة في الابتداء وبين انتهائها إمكان يسع قطع مسافة أقل بتلك السرعة المعينة، وهذا الإمكان أقل من الإمكان الأول، بل جزء منه متأخر عن الجزء الآخر، وكذا بين ابتداء الحركة المتقدمة في الانقطاع وبين انتهائها إمكان يسع قطع مسافة أقل بتلك السرعة المخصوصة وهذا الإمكان أيضاً أقل من الإمكان الأول بل جزء منه متقدم على الجزء الآخر.

قوله : على وجود الزمان أي في الخارج إذ الوهمي ثابت عند الكل كما سيجيء. قوله: إنما نفرض حركة في مسافة اعتبر الشيخ في تقرير هذا البرهان الحركتين المختلفتين في السرعة والبطء متفقتين في الأخذ والترك مع الاختلاف في المسافة، ومتفقتين في الأخذ دون الترك مع اتحاد المسافة ليظهر مغايرة ذلك الإمكان للمسافة حيث اتحد مع اختلاف المسافة في الصورة الأولى، واختلف مع اتحاد المسافة في الصورة الثانية واعتبر تينك الحركتين في نصف مسافتيهما ليظهر قبوله للتجزئة. وبهذا القدر يتم وجود أمر ممتد قابل للزيادة والنقصان فاعتبار الحركتين المتفقتين في السرعة والبطء وفي الأخذ والترك، أو مختلفتين الأخذ والترك كما فعله المصنف مما لا حاجة إليه وقال الكاتبي في شرح الملخص: إن اعتبارهما ليظهر اتصاف ذلك الإمكان بالمساواة، ورده الشارح قدس سره في حواشي شرح الطوالع : بأن ذلك الإمكان هاهنا واحد فلا يوصف بالمساواة لا مقيساً إلى الحركتين وقال ما حاصله أنه إيضاح لقبوله الزيادة والنقصان فإنه إذا كان الحركتان في مرتبة واحدة في السرعة ومتفقتين في الأخذ والترك كانتا متفقتين في ذلك الإمكان، ولو فرض ألف حركة كذلك تكون متفقة معهما في ذلك الإمكان فإذا اختلفتا في الأخذ والترك أو في السرعة والبطء كانتا مختلفتين ذلك الإمكان وأنت خبير بأنه لا يدفع الاستدراك . في قوله: فبين ابتداء إلخ لم يظهر مما تقدم معايرة ذلك الإمكان للمسافة حتى يصح التفريع المذكور. قوله: إمكان عبروا عن ذلك الأمر الممتد بالإمكان لأنه يمكن فيه وقوع تلك المتغيرات وقوعاً أولياً. قوله: فبين ابتداء الحركة المتأخرة إلخ هذا التفريع كالتفريع السابق محل نظر إذ لم يظهر مغايرته للمسافة .
Altogether, the philosophers argued for the existence of time in two ways. The first: we assume a motion in a given distance at a certain speed, and we assume another motion like it in speed. If they start together and end together, they cut that given distance together. So between the beginning of the first fast motion and its end there is an imkan, i.e., an extended thing that accommodates the cutting of that specific distance at that specific speed. Do you not see that the second fast motion, since it shared in that imkan and that speed, also cut the same amount of that distance? And if a thousand motions were assumed in this state, they must be equal in the amount of distance, and it is not permissible for them to differ in that at all. And if one of them starts before, i.e., before the other, and they end together, or one ends before and they start together, the motion that is later in starting on the first estimation, and the motion that is earlier in ending on the second estimation, cuts a smaller distance than the distance of its companion. So between the beginning of the motion that is later in starting and its end there is an imkan that accommodates cutting a smaller distance at that specific speed, and this imkan is less than the first imkan; indeed, it is a part of it that is later than the other part. Similarly, between the beginning of the motion that is earlier in ending and its end there is an imkan that accommodates cutting a smaller distance at that specific speed, and this imkan also is less than the first imkan; indeed, it is a part of it that is prior to the other part.

His statement: 'upon the existence of time' – i.e., in the external world, since the imaginary (al-wahmi) is established for all, as will come. His statement: 'We only assume a motion in a distance' – the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) considered in the formulation of this proof the two motions that differ in speed and slowness, agreeing in inception and cessation with difference in distance, and agreeing in inception without cessation with unity of distance, to show the distinctness of that imkan from distance, since it is one with difference of distance in the first case and differs with unity of distance in the second case. And he considered those two motions in half of their distances to show its susceptibility to division. To this extent, the existence of an extended thing capable of increase and decrease is established. So considering the two motions that agree in speed and slowness and in inception and cessation, or differ in inception and cessation as the author did, is unnecessary. Al-Kātibī said in his commentary on al-Mulakhkhas: that considering them is to show that imkan is characterized by equality. The commentator, may his secret be sanctified, refuted this in his glosses on Sharḥ al-Ṭawāliʿ, by saying that that imkan here is one, so it is not described by equality, not measured against the two motions. And he said, the gist of which is that it is an explanation of its acceptance of increase and decrease: for if the two motions are on one level in speed and agree in inception and cessation, they agree in that imkan; and if a thousand motions were assumed similarly, they would agree with them in that imkan. If they differ in inception and cessation or in speed and slowness, they differ in that imkan. And you are well aware that it does not repel the objection. On his statement: 'so between the beginning, etc.' – it did not appear from what preceded the measurement of that imkan to distance so that the mentioned inference is valid. His statement: 'imkan' – they called that extended thing 'imkan' because it is possible for those changing things to occur in it in a primary occurrence. His statement: 'so between the beginning of the later motion, etc.' – this inference, like the previous inference, is a matter of scrutiny since its distinctness from distance did not appear.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the first face of the philosophers' proof for the existence of time. It begins by assuming two motions of equal speed. If they start and end simultaneously, they cover the same distance, and there is a potentiality (imkan) that is the same for both. If one starts earlier or ends later, the imkan is smaller and is part of the original imkan. The commentator then explains that the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā) used motions differing in speed and slowness to show that the imkan is not identical to distance. Al-Kātibī's objection about equality is refuted by noting that the imkan is one and not characterized by equality; rather, the argument shows the imkan's capacity for increase and decrease. Finally, some inferences are questioned because the distinctness of the imkan from distance is not apparent.

Key Terms
زمان
إمكان
حركة
مسافة
سرعة
بطء
أخذ
ترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this proof concerns physical time as measured by clocks.

Checkpoint
What does the first face of the philosophers' proof compare?
A) Two motions of equal speed with simultaneous or offset starts and ends
B) Two motions of different speeds
C) Two distances of different lengths
D) Two colors of different hues

Key Takeaway
The first proof for time's existence infers an extended potentiality (imkan) from comparing motions of equal speed with different start/end times, showing that this imkan is distinct from distance and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is merely an extended potentiality dependent on motion, does that affect how you perceive the constancy of your daily schedule? What would it mean for your prayers fixed by the sun's motion if the very notion of time is a mental construct?
?
The proof shows that time is not a physical thing but an intellectual measurement. When you feel time 'passing slowly' or 'quickly', are you experiencing reality or your mind's comparison of motions? How does that shape your spiritual sense of eternity?
?
You rely on time to measure your life and deeds. If time is only an intelligible extension, what is the ultimate reference for the value of your actions? Is it the motion of the sun, or something beyond time?

Second Face of the Proof for Time
Al-Jurjani presents the second face of the proof for time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second face of the philosophers' proof demonstrates that the imkan (potential extension) is distinct from distance and is therefore a real entity.

Core Idea
Imkan is not the same as distance; it is a separate extended thing that exists.

Having established the first face, al-Jurjani now presents the second face of the proof for time. This argument considers motions of different speeds that begin and end together. By analyzing the different distances covered, he shows that the imkan (potentiality or extension) cannot be reduced to distance or motion itself. This lesson will walk you through the logical steps of this second face.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms imkan and imkanat. Then read the translation to understand the argument's flow.
The passage uses technical terms like إمكان and امتداد.
2
Identify the assumptions
Notice the assumptions: two motions of different speeds (fast and slow) that start and end at the same time. The fast motion covers a larger distance, the slow a smaller distance.
3
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is that the imkan for each motion differs (one imkan is part of another), and since imkan admits increase, decrease, and division, it must be existent. Understand why pure non-existence cannot have such properties.
This is a key metaphysical principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الانقطاع وبين انتهائها إمكان يسع قطع مسافة أقل بتلك السرعة المخصوصة وهذا الإمكان أيضاً أقل من الإمكان الأول بل جزء منه متقدم على الجزء الآخر وإن اختلفتا في السرعة والبطء واتحدتا في الأخذ والقطع قطعت الحركة السريعة مسافة أكثر من مسافة البطيئة فبين ابتداء هاتين الحركتين وانتهائهما إمكان يسع قطع مسافة أقل ببطء معين ويسع قطع مسافة أكثر بسرعة معينة فإذن هذه الأمور الممتدة التي تسع قطع تلك المسافات إمكانات أي امتدادات تقبل التفاوت، بحيث يكون إمكان جزء لا مكان آخر كما تبين وما كان قابلاً للزيادة والنقصان والتجزئة فهو موجود لأن العدم الصرف لا يكون قابلاً لها بالضرورة وتلخيصه أي تلخيص هذا الوجه وتوضيحه أن الحركة يلحقها تفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان ليس ذلك التفاوت بالمسافة لحصوله أي حصول ذلك التفاوت مع اتحاد المسافة كما إذا قطع سريع وبطيء مسافة واحدة، فإن حركتيهما متفاوتتان في أمر ممتد قطعاً مع تساويهما في المسافة وهذا أعني تساوي المسافة ذلك التفاوت ليس مذكوراً مع في الصور المفروضة المتقدمة وانتفائه أي انتفاء ذلك التفاوت مع تفاوت المسافة كما إذا قطع سريع وبطيء في زمان واحد مسافتين مختلفتين واعترض عليه بأن التفاوت الحاصل مع اتحاد المسافة إنما هو في السرعة والبطء لا في أمر ممتد آخر إلى آخر ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره.
In cessation and between its end there is an imkan that accommodates cutting a smaller distance at that specific speed, and this imkan is also less than the first imkan; indeed, it is a part of it that is prior to the other part. And if they differ in speed and slowness but agree in inception and cessation, the fast motion cuts a larger distance than the slow motion. So between the beginning of these two motions and their ends there is an imkan that accommodates cutting a smaller distance at a given slowness and accommodates cutting a larger distance at a given speed. So then these extended things that accommodate the cutting of those distances are imkanat, i.e., extensions that admit variation, such that one imkan is a part of another imkan as was shown. And whatever is capable of increase, decrease, and division is existent, because pure non-existence cannot be capable of them necessarily. And its summary, i.e., the summary of this face and its clarification, is that motion is subject to variation in increase and decrease. That variation is not due to distance, because that variation occurs with unity of distance, as when a fast and a slow mover cut a single distance; then their two motions surely differ in an extended thing while being equal in distance. And this – I mean the equality of distance – that variation is not mentioned in the previously assumed cases. And its absence, i.e., the absence of that variation, occurs with variation of distance, as when a fast and a slow mover in one time cut two different distances. And objection was raised against it that the variation occurring with unity of distance is only in speed and slowness, not in another extended thing, to the end of what the commentator, may his secret be sanctified, mentioned.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the second face of the philosophers' proof and then summarizes the overall argument. The second face considers motions of different speeds that start and end together. The fast motion covers a greater distance, and the slow motion a shorter distance, but the time (imkan) for each is different. This shows that the imkan is not the same as the distance. The summary then states that motion has variation (increase and decrease) that is not due to distance, because a fast and slow mover on the same distance differ in an extended thing (imkan). Therefore, imkan is something real that admits increase, decrease, and division, hence it is existent. An objection is noted that the variation could be just speed, not a separate entity, but the full reply is in the commentary.

Key Terms
إمكان
امتداد
تفاوت
سرعة
بطء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second face of the proof, why must imkan be existent?
A) Because it is identical to motion.
B) Because it is capable of increase, decrease, and division.
C) Because it is a distance.
D) Because it is a mental construct.

Key Takeaway
The second face shows that imkan is an extended thing that can differ when distances differ, so it is real and distinct from distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is not a mere illusion but a real extended thing that accommodates your actions, how does that change the urgency of how you use each moment? What will you do differently today because time is real and limited?
?
You have just read that imkan is a real entity that varies in size. Your life is a collection of imkans between start and end points. Which 'imkan' in your life right now are you filling with slow, trivial pursuits when you could be filling it with fast, meaningful distance? Name one concrete change.
?
The passage proves that pure non-existence cannot admit variation. Your existence is real and you are constantly changing. Are you actively increasing in good deeds or decreasing? What is one area where you have been stagnant, and what step will you take today to move forward?

Summary and Objection on the Proof for Time
Al-Jurjani summarizes the proof and presents an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the summary of the proof for time, its clarification, and the objection that the variation in motion could be due to speed alone rather than a distinct extended thing.

Core Idea
Motion's variation is not due to distance; an objection questions whether it stems from a separate entity.

After presenting the second face, al-Jurjani provides a concise summary and clarification of the overall proof. He reiterates that the variation in motion (increase and decrease) is not due to distance, as shown by cases where motion differs even with the same distance. However, an objection is raised: perhaps the variation is only in speed and slowness, not in a separate extended thing. This lesson examines the summary and the beginning of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary
Read the Arabic text from 'وتلخيصه' to the end. Understand that the summary states motion is subject to variation that is not due to distance.
The summary restates the core insight.
2
Understand the two cases
The summary mentions two cases: (1) fast and slow mover on the same distance – motion differs in an extended thing; (2) fast and slow mover in the same time on different distances – variation is absent. This shows variation is not from distance.
3
Consider the objection
The objection claims that the variation when distance is the same could be just speed, not another extended thing. Notice how this challenges the foundation of the proof.
The full reply is in the commentary, but here we see the challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الانقطاع وبين انتهائها إمكان يسع قطع مسافة أقل بتلك السرعة المخصوصة وهذا الإمكان أيضاً أقل من الإمكان الأول بل جزء منه متقدم على الجزء الآخر وإن اختلفتا في السرعة والبطء واتحدتا في الأخذ والقطع قطعت الحركة السريعة مسافة أكثر من مسافة البطيئة فبين ابتداء هاتين الحركتين وانتهائهما إمكان يسع قطع مسافة أقل ببطء معين ويسع قطع مسافة أكثر بسرعة معينة فإذن هذه الأمور الممتدة التي تسع قطع تلك المسافات إمكانات أي امتدادات تقبل التفاوت، بحيث يكون إمكان جزء لا مكان آخر كما تبين وما كان قابلاً للزيادة والنقصان والتجزئة فهو موجود لأن العدم الصرف لا يكون قابلاً لها بالضرورة وتلخيصه أي تلخيص هذا الوجه وتوضيحه أن الحركة يلحقها تفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان ليس ذلك التفاوت بالمسافة لحصوله أي حصول ذلك التفاوت مع اتحاد المسافة كما إذا قطع سريع وبطيء مسافة واحدة، فإن حركتيهما متفاوتتان في أمر ممتد قطعاً مع تساويهما في المسافة وهذا أعني تساوي المسافة ذلك التفاوت ليس مذكوراً مع في الصور المفروضة المتقدمة وانتفائه أي انتفاء ذلك التفاوت مع تفاوت المسافة كما إذا قطع سريع وبطيء في زمان واحد مسافتين مختلفتين واعترض عليه بأن التفاوت الحاصل مع اتحاد المسافة إنما هو في السرعة والبطء لا في أمر ممتد آخر إلى آخر ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره.
In cessation and between its end there is an imkan that accommodates cutting a smaller distance at that specific speed, and this imkan is also less than the first imkan; indeed, it is a part of it that is prior to the other part. And if they differ in speed and slowness but agree in inception and cessation, the fast motion cuts a larger distance than the slow motion. So between the beginning of these two motions and their ends there is an imkan that accommodates cutting a smaller distance at a given slowness and accommodates cutting a larger distance at a given speed. So then these extended things that accommodate the cutting of those distances are imkanat, i.e., extensions that admit variation, such that one imkan is a part of another imkan as was shown. And whatever is capable of increase, decrease, and division is existent, because pure non-existence cannot be capable of them necessarily. And its summary, i.e., the summary of this face and its clarification, is that motion is subject to variation in increase and decrease. That variation is not due to distance, because that variation occurs with unity of distance, as when a fast and a slow mover cut a single distance; then their two motions surely differ in an extended thing while being equal in distance. And this – I mean the equality of distance – that variation is not mentioned in the previously assumed cases. And its absence, i.e., the absence of that variation, occurs with variation of distance, as when a fast and a slow mover in one time cut two different distances. And objection was raised against it that the variation occurring with unity of distance is only in speed and slowness, not in another extended thing, to the end of what the commentator, may his secret be sanctified, mentioned.

Commentary
This passage presents the second face of the philosophers' proof and then summarizes the overall argument. The second face considers motions of different speeds that start and end together. The fast motion covers a greater distance, and the slow motion a shorter distance, but the time (imkan) for each is different. This shows that the imkan is not the same as the distance. The summary then states that motion has variation (increase and decrease) that is not due to distance, because a fast and slow mover on the same distance differ in an extended thing (imkan). Therefore, imkan is something real that admits increase, decrease, and division, hence it is existent. An objection is noted that the variation could be just speed, not a separate entity, but the full reply is in the commentary.

زيادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the variation in motion is purely physical speed without any underlying extended reality.
Correction: The proof argues that speed differences themselves imply an extended measure (imkan) that is real, but the objection questions whether this measure is distinct from speed.

Checkpoint
What does the objection claim about the variation when distance is the same?
A) It is due to a different extended thing.
B) It is only in speed and slowness.
C) It is due to distance itself.
D) It is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
The summary clarifies that motion's variation is not from distance, but an objection challenges whether the variation requires a separate entity beyond speed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe differences in your spiritual progress, do you attribute them to external circumstances (like opportunities) or to an internal cause? This lesson argues that variation in motion is not from the path (distance) but from a distinct internal thing. How does this challenge your own assumptions about what drives change in your life?
?
The objection says the variation is 'only in speed and slowness'. Have you ever dismissed the deeper cause behind your actions, reducing them to mere 'faster or slower' performance without asking about the underlying reality of your intentions? What would it look like to search for that 'extended thing' within your own deeds?

Refuting the Objection that Time is Purely Imaginary
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that time is purely imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the claim that time is purely imaginary by showing that it has an external foundation in motion.

Core Idea
Time is the mind's measure of motion, not a pure illusion.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses an objection that time has no reality outside the mind. He draws on Avicenna's al-Shifa to argue that time is grounded in real external motion, though its exact measurement is a mental construct. The commentary clarifies the conditions under which time can be considered existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the opening statement: 'Because pure non-existence... cannot be capable of them.' This sets up the objection that time, being purely imaginary, cannot admit increase and decrease.
Notice the terms 'pure non-existence' (al-ʿadam al-ṣirf) and 'imagination' (tawahhum).
2
Follow the reply from al-Shifa
Read how Avicenna explains that time is impressed in the mind from the relation of a moving body to the two ends of a distance. The mind posits an intermediary measure, which has a basis in external motion.
The key point: time is a mental measure (miqdār) but corresponds to real motion.
3
Understand the implicit criticism
Consider al-Jurjani's comment that the author omitted what was needed. This highlights the nuance that time's existence in the mind does not negate its external foundation.
4
Analyze the final clarification
Examine the discussion on 'capable of increase and decrease'. Al-Jurjani distinguishes between external existence and mental existence, showing that the objection equivocates on the term 'existent'.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : لأن العدم الصرف أي ما لا يكون له وجود لا خارجاً ولا وهماً لا يكون قابلاً لها وليس هذا الوجود له بحسب التوهم، فإنه لو لم يتوهم كان ذلك النحو من الوجود حاصلا كذا الشفاء وفيه بحث؛ لأن من قال بوجوده بالتوهم قال : إن الزمان ينطبع في الذهن من نسبة المتحرك إلى طرفي مسافة الذي هو بقرب أحدهما بالفعل، وليس بقرب الآخر بالفعل إذ حصوله هناك لا يوجد مع حصوله هاهنا في الأعيان، لكن في النفس ويصح في النفس تصورهما وتصور الواسطة بينهما معاً فلا يكون في الأعيان أمر موجود يصل بينهما ويكون في التوهم أمر ينطبع في الذهن أن بين هاهنا وبين وجوده هناك شيئاً في مثله بقطع هذه المسافة بهذه السرعة والبطء الذي لهذه الحركات فيكون هذا تقديراً لتلك الحركة لا وجوداً له، لكن الذهن يوقعه في نفسه لحصول أطراف الحركة فيه بالفعل معاً، كذا في الشفاء والمفهوم منه أن المتحرك في الخارج في حركته بحيث إذا تعقله النفس انتزع فيه ذلك الإمكان وانتفاء التوهم إنما يستلزم انتفاء وجوده بالفعل في النفس لا كون المتحرك بالحيثية المذكورة كما في جميع الأمور الاعتبارية المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر. قوله: وهذا أعني تساوى إلخ تعريض للمصنف بأنه ترك ما يحتاج إليه . قوله: وما كان قابلاً للزيادة والنقصان فهو موجود إن أريد ما كان قابلاً لهما بحسب الخارج موجود فيه فمسلم، لكن قبول تلك الإمكانات إياهما بحسبه ممنوع، وإن أريد ما كان قابلاً لهما في الذهن أو في الجملة موجود في الخارج فممنوع.
His statement: 'Because pure non-existence' – i.e., what has no existence either externally or imaginatively cannot be capable of them. And this existence is not for it in terms of imagination, for if it were not imagined, that kind of existence would still be obtained. Thus in al-Shifāʾ. And there is discussion in it because he who says it exists by imagination says: time is impressed in the mind from the relation of the moving body to the two ends of a distance, where it is close to one end in actuality and not close to the other end in actuality, since its occurrence there does not occur with its occurrence here in external things, but in the soul. And it is valid in the soul to conceive them and to conceive the intermediary between them together. So there is no existing thing in external things that connects them, and there is in imagination a thing that is impressed in the mind that between here and its existence there is a thing like it in cutting this distance at this speed and slowness that these motions have. So this is a measure for that motion, not an existence for it. Rather, the mind posits it in itself because the extremities of motion occur in it together in actuality. Thus in al-Shifāʾ. And what is understood from it is that the moving body in its external motion is such that when the soul reflects on it, it extracts that imkan. And the absence of imagination only entails the absence of its actual existence in the soul, not that the moving body is in the mentioned state, as in all conceptual matters that correspond to what is in the reality of the matter. His statement: 'And this, I mean equality etc.' is an indirect criticism of the author for omitting what he needed. His statement: 'And whatever is capable of increase and decrease is existent' – if what is capable of them in the external world is meant, then it is existent in it, so it is conceded; but the acceptance of those imkanat for them in the external sense is denied. And if what is capable of them in the mind or in general is meant, then it is existent in the external, it is denied.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the existence of time. The objection claims that pure non-existence cannot admit increase and decrease, and that time is only an imaginative construct. The reply from Ibn Sīnā's al-Shifāʾ explains that time is impressed in the mind from the relation of the moving body to the endpoints of a distance. The mind posits an intermediary between the two endpoints, which is a measure, not an external existent. However, it has a foundation in the external motion. The commentator then clarifies: if 'existent' means external, then the imkanat are not externally existent; if it means mental, then they are existent in the mind. This refutes the simple claim that time is purely imaginary.

توهم
ذهن
نفس
أمر اعتباري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a purely subjective illusion with no basis in reality.
Correction: Time is a mental measure that corresponds to real external motion, so it has an external foundation.

Checkpoint

A) Pure imagination without any external reality
B) The relation of the moving body to the two ends of a distance in external motion
C) A direct perception of a separate substance

Key Takeaway
Time is a mental measure grounded in real external motion, not a pure illusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Tusi's Objection to Aristotle's Definition of Time
Raises Tusi's objection that time must precede motion, and answers that the precedence is by essence only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Tusi's famous objection to Aristotle's definition of time and how the response resolves it using the distinction between essential and temporal priority.

Core Idea
Priority by essence is not priority in time.

This lesson examines a famous objection raised by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi against Aristotle's definition of time. The objection seems to create a vicious circle, but a careful distinction between essential and temporal priority resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the core claim: motion requires speed and slowness, which only exist after time is established. Therefore time would need to precede motion.
3
Understand the answer's logical move
The answer says speed and slowness are essential concomitants that are posterior in essence, not in time. Likewise time is a measure posterior in essence.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Contrast temporal priority (earlier in time) with essential priority (logical dependence). The objection mistakenly assumed temporal priority.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ورابعها ما ذهب إليه أرسطو) قيل : يرد عليه السؤال المشهور الذي أورده نصير الدين الطوسي في الأسئلة التي كتبها إلى الكاتبي، وهو أن الحركة لا بد لها من الكيفيتين السرعة والبطء، وذلك إنما هو بعد تقرر الزمان، فقبل حركة الفلك الأعظم زمان هذا خلف والجواب أن إحدى الكيفيتين المذكورتين من لوازم الحركة متأخرة عنها ذاتاً فكذا الزمان لأنه مقدار لها قائم بها فغاية ما لزم تقدم أحد لازميها على الآخر بالذات والمحذور إنما هو تقدم الزمان على حركة الفلك الأعظم ولم يلزم ذلك فليتأمل .
His statement: (And the fourth of them is what Aristotle held.) It is said: there applies to it the famous question raised by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi in the questions he wrote to al-Katibi, namely that motion inevitably has the two qualities of speed and slowness, and that is only after the establishment of time. So before the motion of the celestial sphere, time [exists] — this is a contradiction. And the answer is that one of the two mentioned qualities is among the necessary concomitants of motion, posterior to it in essence. Likewise time, because it is a measure subsisting in it. So the utmost that follows is the priority of one of its two concomitants over the other in essence, but the problematic point is the priority of time over the motion of the celestial sphere, and that does not follow. So reflect.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage records an objection by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi against Aristotle's definition of time as the measure of celestial motion. The objection argues that motion requires speed and slowness, and these only make sense after time is established. So if time is defined as the measure of motion, then time would need to exist before the motion to measure it, which leads to a vicious circle. The answer distinguishes between essential and temporal priority: speed and slowness are essential concomitants of motion, posterior in essence, not in time. Similarly, time as a measure is essentially posterior to the motion it measures, so the objection mistakenly assumes temporal priority when it is merely essential priority. Thus the definition is saved.

Key Terms
سرعة
بطء
لوازم الحركة
تقدم بالذات
مقدار
قائم بها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves time must exist temporally before motion.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the priority is only essential, not temporal. Time is posterior to motion in essence, so there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Tusi's objection against Aristotle's definition of time?
A) That time cannot be a quantity
B) That motion requires speed and slowness, which presuppose time, so time would need to exist before motion
C) That celestial motion is not circular
D) That time is not continuous

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essential and temporal priority saves Aristotle's definition: time is an essential concomitant of motion, not a temporal prerequisite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objection mistakes essential priority for temporal priority. In your life, what do you treat as a prerequisite that is actually a byproduct? How will you reverse that order starting now?

Objection: Time Measures All Motion?
Objection and answer regarding time measuring all motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of time as a measure does not imply it measures all motion, and how it is restricted to the motion of the supreme sphere.

Core Idea
Time's definition excludes non-motion, but specific motion requires other premises.

The commentator anticipates an objection: if time is a measure of motion, doesn't it have to measure every motion? This seems to contradict the claim that time is specifically the measure of the supreme sphere's motion. The answer clarifies the intention of the definition and refers to other premises already established.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the objection: the definition would make time a measure of all motion, but philosophers say it is only for the supreme sphere.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer states the definition only aims to exclude non-motion, not to specify all motion. The specific connection to the supreme sphere comes from other premises.
These premises were explained earlier – recall that time is linked to celestial motion.
3
Connect to preceding premises
Reflect on why the commentator says 'so no defect' – the objection fails because the definition alone doesn't commit to all motions; it's a general framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقد يوجه ذلك القول) فإن قلت: هذا التوجيه يقتضي أن يكون الزمان مقداراً للحركة مطلقاً سواء كانت مستقيمة أو مستديرة وقد صرحوا بأنه مقدار لحركة الفلك الأعظم قلت : المقصود من هذا التوجيه أنه لا يلزم أن يكون مقداراً لغير الحركة، وأما كونه مقدار الحركة مخصوصة أعني حركة الفلك الأعظم فمقدمات أخر قد سلف بيانها فلا قصور فإن قلت : نفس الزمان مما له هوية اتصالية غير قارة فيلزم أن يكون له زمان آخر على مقتضى كلامه قلت : بعد تسليم لزوم الزمان الزائد مراده من الموجود المذكور غير الزمان وإنما سكت عن استثنائه لما سبق من بيانه غير مرة .
His statement: 'And it may be directed that statement' — for if you say: this directing entails that time is a measure for motion absolutely, whether rectilinear or circular, whereas they have stated that it is a measure for the motion of the supreme sphere. I say: the purpose of this directing is that it does not necessarily become a measure for non-motion. As for its being a measure for a specific motion, namely the motion of the supreme sphere, there are other premises whose explanation has preceded, so no defect. For if you say: time itself is among things that have a continuous non-fixed identity, so it would require another time according to his statement. I say: after conceding the necessity of an additional time, his intention by the mentioned existent is other than time; he only omitted its exception because of what has been explained more than once.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. It presents two objections. First objection: the definition implies time measures all motion, but philosophers say it is only for the motion of the supreme sphere. Answer: the definition only aims to show time does not measure non-motion; its being specific to celestial motion is from other premises already established. Second objection: time itself has a continuous non-fixed identity, so it would require another time. Answer: the speaker meant the existent other than time; he omitted this exception because it had been explained multiple times.

Key Terms
حركة مستقيمة
حركة مستديرة
حركة الفلك الأعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time must measure all motion because it is defined as a measure of motion.
Correction: The definition only shows time is not a measure of non-motion; its specific connection to celestial motion is from other arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of defining time as a measure of motion according to the answer?
A) To show it measures all motion.
B) To exclude it from measuring non-motion.
C) To prove it is only for circular motion.
D) To reject the definition.

Key Takeaway
Time's definition as a measure of motion only excludes non-motion; its specific reference to the supreme sphere's motion comes from other premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume a definition applies universally, what hidden premises might you be ignoring? This lesson shows that even a correct definition can be misapplied without supporting truths. In your own understanding of time and destiny, have you ever oversimplified a deep truth by clinging to a definition instead of investigating the full argument? Will you commit today to exploring one theological concept more deeply, beyond its surface definition?
?
The objection in this lesson arises from an overgeneralization. In your daily life, where do you commit the same error—applying a partial truth as if it were the whole? Consider your judgments of others or your understanding of God's decree. Can you identify one area where you must distinguish between a general principle and its specific application, and then act on that distinction?

Objection: Time Requiring Another Time
Objection and answer regarding time requiring another time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the objection that time itself would require another time due to its continuous non-fixed identity.

Core Idea
Time does not need another time because the existent meant is not time.

A second objection argues that if time has a continuous non-fixed identity, then it would itself need a time to measure it, leading to an infinite regress. The answer reveals that the 'existent' referred to in the definition is not time but something else, an exception already explained.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the objection: time's continuous non-fixed identity would require another time. The answer concedes the necessity but says the intended existent is other than time.
2
Analyze the answer's concession
The answer says 'after conceding the necessity of an additional time' – this does not mean time actually requires another time; it is a conditional concession to show the objection fails.
3
Understand the exception
The commentator explains that the speaker meant something other than time by 'the existent', and this exception was omitted for brevity because it had been explained before.
This exception likely refers to the continuous non-fixed identity being attributed to another thing, not time itself.

Source Text

This passage is an objection-and-answer section. It presents two objections. First objection: the definition implies time measures all motion, but philosophers say it is only for the motion of the supreme sphere. Answer: the definition only aims to show time does not measure non-motion; its being specific to celestial motion is from other premises already established. Time Cannot Be the Measure of Motion
Argument against time as measure of motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time cannot be the measure of motion in either of its two senses.

Core Idea
Time fails as a measure of motion for both meanings of motion.

The author presents a two-pronged argument against the Ash'arite view that time is the measure of motion. By distinguishing two senses of motion (the continuous 'being in the middle' and the non-existent 'extended'), he shows that time cannot serve as a measure in either case, undermining the Ash'arite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own progression come through.
Notice that the passage begins with 'Second', indicating it is part of a larger argument.
2
Identify the two senses of motion
The passage states that motion is said in two ways: 'being in the middle' (al-kawn fi al-wasat) and 'the extended' (al-mumtadda). Understand each definition.
Al-kawn fi al-wasat is a continuous thing from start to end; al-mumtadda has no external existence.
3
Analyse the argument for each sense
For the first sense, if time were its measure, time would be fixed, contradicting its non-fixed nature. For the second sense, if time were its measure, time would have no external existence, contradicting its real existence.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum for each case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أن الحركة كما مر ( تقال للكون في الوسط ) أعني ما بين المبدأ والمنتهى (وهو) أي الكون في الوسط ( أمر مستمر من المبدأ إلى المنتهى، ولو كان الزمان مقداره كان ثابتاً مثله فلا يكون مقداراً غير قار كما ذهبتم إليه (و) يقال أيضاً ( للممتدة من المبدأ إلى المنتهى ولا وجود لها في الخارج (اتفاقاً وبالضرورة أيضاً، كما مر ( فلو كان ( الزمان ( مقدارها لم يوجد ) الزمان في الخارج أصلاً فلا يكون مقداراً موجوداً في الخارج قائماً بالحركة كما هو مذهبكم
Second: motion, as was mentioned, is said of 'being in the middle' (al-kawn fi al-wasat), i.e., what is between the beginning and the end. And it (i.e., being in the middle) is a continuous thing from the beginning to the end. If time were its measure, it would be fixed like it, so it would not be a non-fixed measure as you claim. And it is also said of 'the extender' (al-mumtadda) from the beginning to the end, and it has no existence in the external world (by agreement and necessarily, as was mentioned). So if time were its measure, time would not exist in the external world at all, so it would not be an existing measure subsisting in motion as is your doctrine.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a proof by disjunction. Motion is said in two senses. First, as 'al-kawn fi al-wasat' (being in the middle), which is a continuous thing from start to end. If time were its measure, time would be fixed (like that continuous thing), contradicting the required non-fixed nature of time. Second, as 'al-mumtadda' (the extended), which has no external existence. If time were its measure, time would also have no external existence, contradicting the claim that time is an existing measure subsisting in motion. Thus, time cannot be the measure of motion in either sense.

Key Terms
الكون في الوسط
الممتدة
الحركة
ثابت
غير قار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion has only one meaning.
Correction: The author distinguishes two meanings of motion: 'being in the middle' (al-kawn fi al-wasat) and 'the extended' (al-mumtadda).

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if motion is taken as the continuous extent (الممتدة), what would be the consequence for time?
A) Time would be fixed.
B) Time would not exist externally.
C) Time would be non-fixed.
D) Time would be the same as motion.

Key Takeaway
Time cannot be the measure of motion because neither sense of motion allows time to be a non-fixed, existing measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You measure your life by time—years, days, hours. But if time cannot even measure motion, on what basis are you measuring your spiritual journey? Are you using a false metric, like the number of prayers or years of practice, while ignoring the inner reality that only Allah knows? This lesson forces you to ask: what is the true measure of your nearness to Him?
?
The passage says that the 'extended' motion has no external existence—it is only conceptual. How many of your religious resolutions and aspirations exist only in your mind, never becoming real actions? Examine your heart: are you content with a mere mental 'motion' while your limbs remain stationary? Let this passage wake you to the need for real, external obedience.
?
Time is fixed if it measures the fixed state of being in the middle. You often think your spiritual state is stable—'I am a good Muslim'—but that sense of stability may be deceptive. If time is not the measure, then your fixed state is not guaranteed; you are in constant need of renewal. Will you rely on your own perception of stability, or will you seek the never-fixed but ever-renewing grace of Allah?

Place is not part of the thing located, with a logical objection
Definition of place and logical critique of a proposed proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why place is not considered a part of the thing located and why one attempted proof fails on logical grounds.

Core Idea
Place is not a part of what it contains, and flawed logic cannot prove otherwise.

This lesson examines a key definition in classical physics: place (al-makan) belongs to the category of continuous quantity and is not a part of the located object. The author also critiques a logical argument attempting to equate place with matter, pointing out an invalid syllogistic form and a false premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and initial claim
Notice how the passage begins by stating what place is (continuous quantity) and asserts it is not part of the located object.
The author uses a conditional: if place were part, it would move with the object.
2
Understand the logical objection
The author points out that if place were part of the located object, it would move with it. Then he notes that a previous argument using two affirmative premises in the second figure is invalid.
The second figure with two affirmatives does not produce a valid conclusion.
3
Examine the attempted correction and its rebuttal
See how the corrected argument fails because its major premise is clearly false. Reflect on the reasoning.
The major premise 'whatever is succeeded by multiple things is matter' is rejected as obviously false.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل
المكان ليس جزءاً من المتمكن وإلا انتقل بانتقاله وعرفت أنه) أي الشأن (لا ينتج الموجبتان في الشكل الثاني وما ذكره من هذا القبيل كما ترى ولو أريد إصلاحه بأن يقال : المكان يتعاقب عليه المتمكنات وكل ما يتعاقب عليه أشياء متعددة فهو الهيولى كانت الكبرى ظاهرة الكذب
The second observation - the ninth purpose: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. Place is not a part of the thing located, otherwise it would move with its movement. And you have known that (i.e., the matter) two affirmative propositions in the second figure do not produce a conclusion, and what he mentioned is of this sort, as you see. And if one wanted to correct it by saying: place is succeeded by things located, and everything that is succeeded by multiple things is matter, then the major premise would be clearly false.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage defines place and critiques an attempted proof. It begins by stating that place is not a part of the thing located (al-mutamakkin), because if it were, it would move with it. Then it notes a logical issue: the proposed argument uses two affirmative premises in the second figure of syllogism, which does not yield a conclusion. An attempted correction is offered—that place is succeeded by things located, and whatever is succeeded by multiple things is matter (hayula)—but the author points out that the major premise in this corrected version is obviously false. Thus the definition stands, and the attempted identification of place with matter fails.

Plato's View on Place as Matter and Its Logical Critique
Plato's identification of place with matter, an alternative interpretation as corporeal form, and logical/doxographical objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Plato's opinion that place is matter, the alternative that place is corporeal form, and the logical issues with the argument using the second figure.

Core Idea
Plato's view of place as matter highlights the subtle interplay between philosophy and logic in classical Islamic thought.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents Plato's opinion that place is matter (hayula), along with a logical argument and its critique. It also clarifies Plato's actual doctrine on the composition of body, showing a nuanced transmission of his ideas. Let's dive into the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense commentary style.
2
Identify the two views on place
The passage presents two opinions: that place is matter (hayula) and that place is corporeal form. Find where each is stated.
3
Analyze the logical issue
The passage criticizes the argument for place being matter as a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative premises. Understand why this is problematic.
The middle term must be repeated; here it is not.
4
Understand Plato's actual doctrine
The commentary clarifies that Plato did not hold matter-form composition for bodies. Body is a simple substance; matter and form are names for its receptive and determining aspects.
This resolves the apparent inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the commentator harmonizes Plato's statement with his known doctrine through alternative transmission or concessive interpretation.

And this opinion is attributed to Plato, and perhaps he used the term 'matter' for it, i.e., for place, by homonymy with the presence of suitability between place and matter in the succession of things upon them; otherwise, the impossibility of matter being a part of the body is something that is not unclear to an intelligent person, let alone one like him in his intelligence. His statement: (That it be said: Place is succeeded by things located) He did not intend by 'things located' things located, such that it would be objected that this is not a correction of the proof due to the non-repetition of the middle term, since 'multiple things' is more general than 'things located', so it becomes like our saying: Zaid is described as human, and everything described as animal is walking, and there is no doubt about its lack of regularity; rather he intended by it 'the multiple things', so if he replaced it with 'multiple things' it would be better. His statement: (Otherwise the impossibility of matter being a part of the body, etc.) If you say: Plato does not say that body is composed of matter and form; rather body according to him is a simple substance, and matter according to him is a name for the body in terms of its receiving the accidental configurations that determine bodies and diversify them, and form is a name for those accidents, so his statement 'which is a part of the body' is inappropriate for the context. I say: The apparent meaning of his statement in the inference 'matter accepts the succession of bodies, i.e., the corporeal form' indicates that there is another transmission from him different from what is well-known of his doctrine, or his speech is interpreted as a concession, given that the commentator's speech is not explicit that the part is according to his doctrine. Reflect.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage discusses Plato's attribution of place to matter (hayula) and the logical argument used. It begins by noting that Plato might have used the term 'matter' for place by homonymy due to the similarity of succession of things upon both. Then it mentions another opinion that place is the corporeal form because it is a determiner and container. The passage includes extensive commentary clarifying Plato's actual doctrine: he did not hold that body is composed of matter and form; rather body is a simple substance, and matter is the name for the body in terms of receiving determining accidents, while form is the name for those accidents. The logical issues about the middle term and the second figure are also addressed. The commentator defends Plato against the charge of inconsistency by suggesting a different transmission or a concessive interpretation.

Refuting the Claim That Place Is Form
Refutation of the claim that place is form, with reference to Plato's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that place is form because it is a determiner encompassing by itself fails, and how two distinct essences can share a concomitant without being identical.

Core Idea
Sharing a property does not prove identity.

The opinion that place is form relies on a logical step: place is a determiner encompassing, and every such determiner is form, so place is form. But is the second premise true? This lesson unpacks the critique: two different things can share a common property without being identical. The passage also touches on Plato's alternative view, redirecting to the core question.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its rebuttal
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: a claim, an added judgment, and then a refutation.
The added judgment is the major premise 'every determiner encompassing by itself is form'.
2
Understand the logical mistake
Focus on the refutation: two distinct essences can share a single concomitant. Therefore, sharing the property 'determiner encompassing by itself' does not make place and form identical. The major premise is false.
This is the key insight: a shared property does not entail identity.
3
See how Plato's view fits in
The passage mentions that Plato identified place with space/abstract dimension, sometimes calling it matter or form. But these interpretations either reduce to the later correct view or are false. The real issue is whether place is dimension or something else.
This sets up the next discussion about the nature of place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل
مذهبه،
المكان محدد حاو بالذات، وكل محدد حاو بالذات هو الصورة لكن هذا الحكم المزيد غير مسلم. وإليه أشار بقوله ( ويبطل ) أي هذا الحكم الذي زيد بأن الذاتين المتباينتين قد يشتركان في لازم واحد فلا يلزم من ذلك صدق إحداهما على الأخرى فضلاً عن اتحادهما فتكون الكبرى حينئذ ممنوعة الصدق. وهذا المذهب أيضاً ينسب إلى أفلاطون قالوا : لما ذهب إلى أن المكان هو الفضاء والبعد المجرد سماء تارة بالهيولى لما سبق من المناسبة وأخرى بالصورة لأن الجواهر الجسمانية قابلة له بنفوذه فيها دون الجواهر المجردة فهو الجزء الصوري للأجسام فهذان القولان إن حملا على هذا الذي ذكرناه فقد رجعا إلى ما سيأتي من وإلا فلا اعتداد بهما لظهور بطلانهما، وإنما الاشتباه في أن المكان هو البعد أو غيره
The second observation - the ninth purpose: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. His doctrine? Place is a determiner encompassing by itself, and every determiner encompassing by itself is the form. But this added judgment is not conceded. And to it he pointed by his saying (and it is invalidated), i.e., this judgment that was added because two distinct essences may share a single concomitant, so that does not entail the truth of one upon the other, let alone their identity. Thus the major premise would then be denied truth. And this opinion is also attributed to Plato: they said: since he held that place is space and abstract dimension, he named it sometimes matter due to the aforementioned suitability, and other times form because corporeal substances are receptive to it by its penetrating into them unlike immaterial substances, so it is the formal part of bodies. These two statements, if interpreted according to what we have mentioned, then they reduce to what will come later; otherwise, no attention is paid to them due to the apparent falsity of both. And the confusion is only whether place is dimension or something else.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage continues the critique of the second opinion (place as form). It states the argument: place is a determiner encompassing by itself, and every such determiner is the form, so place is form. But the added judgment (that every determiner encompassing by itself is the form) is not conceded. The reason is that two distinct essences can share a single concomitant, so it does not imply identity. The major premise is therefore false. Then it mentions that this opinion (place as form) is also attributed to Plato, who said place is space and abstract dimension, sometimes called matter, sometimes form. However, these two opinions, if interpreted correctly, reduce to what will come later (the definition of place as dimension or surface); otherwise they are invalid. The real issue is whether place is dimension or something else.

الذاتين المتباينتين
لازم واحد

Correction: Two distinct essences can share a single concomitant without being identical, so sharing 'determiner encompassing by itself' does not prove place is form.

Checkpoint

A) Because place is not a determiner
B) Because two distinct essences can share a single concomitant without being identical
C) Because the form is not a determiner
D) Because the argument uses the second figure incorrectly

Key Takeaway
The claim that place is form fails because two distinct things can share a property without being identical; the real debate is whether place is dimension or something else.

Body's Relation to Place: Contact Types and the Three Possibilities for Place
Classification of place as dimension or surface, with three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of contact between body and place, and the resulting three possibilities for what place is: existent dimension, imaginary dimension, or the inner surface of the container.

Core Idea
Place is either dimension or surface; dimension is either real or imagined.

This passage explores the nature of place through the relation of body to its place. It argues that the only conceivable relation is contact, which comes in two forms: complete interpenetration or partial contiguity. From this, place is defined either as the dimension penetrated or the surface that touches, leading to three possibilities for place's existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial description
Read the first part describing that the body fills its place and the place surrounds it. This sets up the need for contact.
2
Identify the two types of contact
Notice that contact can be complete (mudakhala) or by extremities (mumassa). Complete contact implies place as dimension; partial contact implies place as surface.
3
Derive the three possibilities
From the two types, place is either dimension or surface. Then dimension itself can be existent or imaginary, giving three possibilities.
The text then provides a refutation of other views, but the main point is this classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فشرع يتكلم عليه فقال : ( ثم الجسم منطبق على مكانه ) الحقيقي ليس زائداً عليه ( مالئ له ) ليس ناقصاً عنه بحيث لا يخلو شيء من مكانه عنه ( والمكان محيط به ) أي هوي بتمامه في المكان ليس شيء منه خارجاً عنه، ولهذا ينسب إليه بكلمة في مملوء منه كما ذكرناه وقد عرفت أنه يجوز انتقاله عنه ( ولا يتصور ذلك ) المذكور من حال الجسم ومكانه بالقياس إلى صاحبه ) إلا بالملاقاة ) بينهما وتلك الملاقاة ( إما بالتمام ) بحيث إذا فرض جزء من المتمكن يفرض بإزائه جزء من المكان وبالعكس فيتطابقان بالكلية . ( وتسمى ( الملاقاة على هذا الوجه ( المداخلة فيكون ) المكان على هذا التقدير ) هو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه الجسم، وينطبق البعد الحال فيه على ذلك البعد في أعماقه وأقطاره ) وإما لا بالتمام بل بالأطراف ) أي تكون أطراف الجسم ملاقية لمكانه دون أعماقه ) تسمى ( الملاقاة على هذا الوجه ( المماسة فيكون المكان حينئذ ( هو السطح الباطن للحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى، فإذن المكان إما البعد وإما سطح (الحاوي لا ثالث لهما، فإذا بطل أحدهما تعين الثاني، والبعد إما موجود أو مفروض موهوم ) فهذه ثلاثة احتمالات ) لا
قوله : ( لظهور بطلانهما في الشفاء، أما بيان فساد من يرى أن الهيولى والصورة مكان، فبأن يعلم أن المكان يفارق عند الحركة والهيولى والصورة لا يفارقان والمكان تكون الحركة فيه، والهيولى والصورة لا يكون الحركة فيهما بل معهما، والمكان يكون إليه الحركة، والهيولى والصورة لا يكون إليهما الحركة البتة والمتكون إذا تكون استبدل مكانه الطبيعي كالماء إذا صار هواء، ولا تستبدل هيولاه الطبيعية وفي ابتداء الكون يكون في المكان الأول ولا يكون في صورته . ويقال: إنَّ الخشب كان سريراً. ويقال : إنَّ الماء كان بخاراً، وإن النطفة كان إنساناً، ولا يقال : إنه المكان كان جسماً كذا. قوله : ( والبعد إما موجود أو مفروض (موهوم أي مع قطع النظر عن دلائل الوجود .
Then he began to speak about it and said: (Then the body is applied to its place) the real one, not exceeding it (filling it) not falling short of it such that nothing is empty of its place from it. (And the place surrounds it) i.e., it is entirely in the place, nothing of it is outside it. For this reason it is attributed to it by the word 'in' as we mentioned, and you have known that it is permissible for it to move from it. (And that is not conceivable) the mentioned condition of body and its place in relation to its owner except by contact (mulaqa) between them. And that contact is either complete (bi-al-tamam), such that if a part of the located thing is assumed, a part of the place is assumed opposite it, and vice versa, so they correspond entirely. (And it is called) contact in this manner (interpenetration, mudakhala). Thus (place on this estimation) is the dimension (bu'd) in which the body penetrates, and the dimension residing in it coincides with that dimension in its depths and extremities. Or it is not complete but rather by extremities, i.e., the extremities of the body are in contact with its place without its depths. It is called (contact in this manner) (contiguity, mumassa). So place then (is the inner surface of the container that touches the outer surface of the contained). Thus place is either dimension or the surface of the container; no third for them. If one is invalidated, the other is determined. And dimension is either existent or assumed imaginary. So these are three possibilities. No. His statement: (Because of the apparent falsity of both in al-Shifa): As for the demonstration of the corruption of the one who holds that matter and form are place, it is by knowing that place separates upon motion while matter and form do not separate; motion occurs in place, while matter and form do not have motion in them but rather with them; motion is towards place, while matter and form are never towards motion; and when something comes into being, it replaces its natural place, like water when it becomes air, but it does not replace its natural matter; and at the beginning of coming into being it has the primary place but not its form. It is said: The wood was a bed. It is said: The water was steam. It is said: The sperm was a human. And it is not said: The place was such a body. His statement: (And dimension is either existent or assumed imaginary) i.e., setting aside the proofs of existence.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from the relation of body to its place. It begins by stating that the body is applied to its true place, filling it and not exceeding it, and the place surrounds it. This relation can only be conceived through contact, which is of two types: complete contact (interpenetration, mudakhala), where the body and place coincide in all parts, leading to the definition of place as the dimension in which the body penetrates; and partial contact by extremities (contiguity, mumassa), where only the boundaries touch, leading to place as the inner surface of the container. Therefore, place is either dimension (bu'd) or surface (sath). Dimension itself is either existent (mawjud) or assumed imaginary (mafrud mawhum). Thus there are three possibilities. The passage then includes a quotation from al-Shifa that provides additional arguments against the views that place is matter or form: place separates upon motion, motion is in place, motion is towards place, and bodies replace their natural place but not their matter or form. The final note clarifies that 'existent or assumed imaginary' means setting aside proofs of existence.

Key Terms
الجسم منطبق على مكانه
الملاقاة
المداخلة
المماسة
البعد
سطح الحاوي
موجود
مفروض موهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that place is matter or form.
Correction: The passage refutes this by showing that place separates upon motion, motion occurs in place, motion is towards place, and bodies replace their natural place but not their matter or form, thus place cannot be matter or form.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what are the three possibilities for place?
A) Existent dimension, imaginary dimension, surface of the container
B) Matter, form, dimension
C) Interpenetration, contiguity, vacuum
D) Physical place, mathematical place, spiritual place

Key Takeaway
Place is definitively either dimension (real or imagined) or the inner surface of a container, based on the nature of contact between body and place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that place completely surrounds a body and is filled by it. In your life, what 'place'—be it your heart, your time, or your priorities—is not being fully filled by your worship? Where are the gaps that leave room for negligence? Will you commit today to fill that space completely with remembrance of Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes between interpenetration and contiguity. In your relationship with Allah, do you approach Him only at the surface (contiguity), observing rituals externally, or do you allow His commands to penetrate your depths (interpenetration)? What would it mean for you to let His guidance fill every part of your being?
?
Place is either real dimension or assumed imaginary. How much of your identity is grounded in the 'imaginary' dimensions of this world—status, possessions, opinions? If your true place is with Allah, are you living as if that is your real dimension, or are you attached to fleeting illusions? What can you change today to anchor yourself in the real?

The Three Subcases of Container and Contained Motion
Al-Jurjani enumerates three subcases of place motion and refines the example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three potential motions of a body and its place (container and contained) and the refined example used to avoid the vacuum objection.

Core Idea
A body and its place can move together, container alone, or contained alone—but never with vacuum.

Al-Jurjani continues his analysis of place (makan) by examining the third branch: the motion of the container (al-hawi) and the contained (al-mahwi). He lists three subcases and then addresses an objection by providing a more precise example using nested spheres. This lesson will walk through these subcases and the reasoning behind the alternative example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the three subcases are listed, then an objection is raised, and finally a refined example is given.
2
Identify the three subcases
Find the three possibilities: (1) both container and contained move together (concordant or discordant), (2) container alone moves, (3) contained alone moves. Note the bird and wind examples.
The third branch of place motion distinguishes these cases.
3
Understand the vacuum objection
When the bird moves, air must move to avoid vacuum. This makes it seem that the container (air) also moves, blurring the case of 'contained alone'. Why is this a problem for the simple examples?
Proponents of surface theory (asthab al-sath) reject vacuum.
4
Appreciate the refined example
Al-Jurjani offers a better example: three concentric spheres; the middle one moves alone. This clearly illustrates both 'container alone' and 'contained alone' without involving vacuum.
The sphere's convexity touches another's concavity, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد التاسع : المكان وهو من الكم المتصل (الثالث) من تلك الفروع ( أنه قد يتحرك الحاوي والمحوي معاً) إما متوافقين في الجهة أو متخالفين فيها ) كالطير يطير والريح تهب ) على الوفاق أو الخلاف (أو) يتحرك (الحاوي وحده كالطير يقف والريح تهب أو) يتحرك ( المحوي وحده كالطير يطير والريح تقف ) . وقد يقال : إذا تحرك الطير: انخرق الهواء قدامه والتأم من خلفه . إذ لا يجوز الخلاء عند أصحاب السطح فيلزم تحرك الهواء من تحرك الطير فالأولى أن يمثل بكرة تماس بمحدبها مقعر كرة أخرى وبمقعرها محدب كرة ثالثة وتكون المتوسطة متحركة وحدها فيكون مثالاً لكل واحدة من حركتي الحاوي والمحوي وحده
The second observatory - the ninth aim: Place, and it is of continuous quantity. (The third) of those branches (is that the container and the contained may move together) either concordant in direction or discordant, like a bird flying and wind blowing, whether concordant or discordant; (or) the container alone moves, like a bird standing and wind blowing; or the contained alone moves, like a bird flying and wind still. And it may be said: when the bird moves, the air ahead of it is torn and behind it is sealed, since vacuum is not allowed among the proponents of [the theory of] surface, so the air necessarily moves from the motion of the bird. So it is better to exemplify with a sphere whose convexity touches the concavity of another sphere and whose concavity touches the convexity of a third sphere, the middle sphere moving alone, so that it is an example for each of the two motions: of the container alone and of the contained alone.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the third branch (الثالث) from the earlier classification of branches concerning the motion of place (al-makan). The commentator specifies three subcases: (1) both the container (al-ḥāwī) and the contained (al-maḥwī) move together, either in the same direction or opposite; (2) the container alone moves while the contained is stationary; (3) the contained alone moves while the container is stationary. Examples are given: a bird flying and wind blowing for the first, bird still and wind blowing for the second, bird flying and wind still for the third. The commentator then notes an objection: if the bird moves, the air must also move (since no vacuum exists), so it appears the container also moves. To avoid this, a better example is given using three concentric spheres: the middle sphere moving alone illustrates both cases of container or contained moving alone.

Key Terms
حاوي
محوي
حركة
سطح
خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third branch is about the body itself moving, not about the place (surface or dimension) moving.
Correction: The branch concerns the motion of the place, i.e., the container (like air or surface) and the contained (the body). The subcases classify how the container and contained can move relative to each other.

Checkpoint
In the third branch, which of the following is NOT a subcase?
A) Both container and contained move together
B) Container alone moves
C) Contained alone moves
D) Neither moves

Key Takeaway
The three subcases cover all possibilities of motion between a body and its place, and the sphere example elegantly avoids the vacuum objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, the bird example seemed correct but was flawed because the motion of the bird necessarily moves the air. In your life, are there actions you take that you think only affect you, but actually move your 'container'—your family, community, or environment? Are you willing to examine those actions and their hidden consequences?
?
Al-Jurjani refined his example to avoid the vacuum problem. What is a belief or assumption you hold about your own spiritual state that you have never critically examined? Will you allow this lesson to prompt you to refine that understanding, no matter how uncomfortable it is?
