Al-Razi's Definition of Moisture
Al-Razi defines moisture and defends it against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's definition of moisture as a quality that necessitates ease of adhesion and separation, and how it withstands a potential objection.

Core Idea
Moisture is not adhesion itself but the quality that makes adhesion and parting easy.

Imam al-Razi offers a precise definition of moisture that distinguishes it from mere adhesion. He argues that moisture is a quality (kayfiyya) that underlies the observed ease of sticking and separating. This definition, though metaphorical, avoids common pitfalls and clarifies physical categorization. Read on to see how al-Razi defends his view against a challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two parts: definition and defense.
2
Identify the definition and its nuance
Al-Razi defines moisture as a quality that necessitates ease of adhesion and separation. Note that this is a metaphorical expression: moisture is not the ease itself but a quality that causes it.
3
Follow the objection and response
An objector claims that if moisture were ease of adhesion, then a finely ground dry substance would be moist. Al-Razi's response: the adhesion there is due to smallness, not moisture, and the objector's own premises lead to inconsistency.
This shows how the definition avoids unwanted consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الرطوبة واليبوسة وفيهما مباحث . أحدها : الرطوبة سهولة الالتصاق ) أي كيفية تقتضي سهولة الالتصاق بالغير ( و ) سهولة ( الانفصال ) عنه هذا هو المختار في تفسير الرطوبة عند الإمام الرازي

قوله : ( أي كيفية إلخ) يعني أن تفسير الرطوبة بما ذكر قول مجازي لان الالتصاق وسهولته من الإضافة والرطوبة ليست منها والمراد كيفية تقتضي ذلك فلا يرد ما قيل : إِنَّ الرطوبة لو كانت عبارة عن سهولة الالتصاق لوجب أن يكون اليابس المدقوق دقاً ناعماً رطباً لكونه كذلك لا في سهولة التصاقه بسبب تصغر أجزائه، والتصغر ليست بكيفية. وأما ما قيل من أن التصاقه بواسطة مخالطة الأجزاء الهوائية فليس بشيء، لأن من فسر الرطوبة بسهولة الالتصاق لا يقول برطوبة الهواء فلا هو يصح هذا الجواب من قبله
Concerning moisture and dryness, and in them are investigations. One of them: Moisture is ease of adhesion – i.e., a quality that necessitates ease of adhesion to another and ease of separation from it. This is the chosen interpretation of moisture according to Imam al-Razi.

His saying (i.e., 'a quality etc.'): It means that the interpretation of moisture by what was mentioned is a metaphorical expression, because adhesion and its ease are from the category of relation (al-idafa), and moisture is not from it. The intended meaning is a quality that necessitates that. So what was said does not apply: that if moisture were an expression for ease of adhesion, then it would be necessary that a dry substance finely ground would be moist, because it is likewise due to the smallness of its parts, and smallness is not a quality. As for what was said, that its adhesion is due to the mixture of airy parts, it is nothing, because whoever interprets moisture as ease of adhesion does not affirm the moisture of air. So this answer is not valid from him.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage defines moisture (ruthuba) as a quality (kayfiyya) that necessitates ease of adhesion (suhulat al-iltisaq) to another body and ease of separation (suhulat al-infiṣal) from it, and states that this is the chosen definition according to Imam al-Razi. The commentary clarifies that the definition is metaphorical: adhesion and its ease are relational categories (al-idafa), not a quality itself, but the quality necessitates that relational ease. A potential objection is raised: if moisture is ease of adhesion, then a finely ground dry substance (like powdered bone) would be considered moist because it adheres easily due to small parts. The response explains that smallness is not a quality, and the adhesion there is due to mixture with airy parts, but those who define moisture this way do not affirm the moisture of air, so the objection fails. The passage thus defends the definition by showing it does not entail unwanted consequences.

Key Terms
رطوبة
سهولة الالتصاق
سهولة الانفصال
كيفية
الإمام الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moisture is simply the ease of adhesion itself.
Correction: Moisture is a quality that causes ease of adhesion, not the ease itself, which is a relational attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi's definition, what is moisture?
A) Adhesion to other bodies
B) Ease of adhesion and separation
C) The quality of being wet
D) The opposite of dryness

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi defines moisture as a quality that necessitates ease of adhesion and separation, a definition that holds against a key objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever define things by their effects rather than their essence, as some mistakenly define moisture as mere ease of adhesion? Consider one belief you hold—are you sure you have grasped its true nature, or have you confused the symptom with the cause? Commit to examining that belief with the same precision al-Razi used.
?
Al-Razi defended his definition against an objection. When your faith or understanding is challenged, do you engage with the objection honestly or rely on weak excuses? Think of a recent doubt or criticism you faced. Did you respond with a solid defense or just dismiss it? Resolve to learn the foundational proofs of your beliefs so that you can answer with clarity.

Ibn Sina's Objection to the Definition of Moisture and the Response
Ibn Sina objects that honey would be more moist than water, and the response distinguishes intensity from ease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's objection to defining moisture as adhesion, the response distinguishing intensity from ease, and the alternative definition involving ease of receiving shapes.

Core Idea
Moisture is ease of adhesion and separation, not intensity of adhesion.

Ibn Sina challenged the common definition of moisture as adhesion, arguing that honey and oil would then be more moist than water, which is false. Imam al-Razi refined the definition to include ease of adhesion and separation. Later commentators also considered ease of receiving shapes. This lesson examines that debate and the responses to objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read Ibn Sina's objection in the source passage and Imam al-Razi's response. Notice how the response shifts the definition from adhesion itself to ease of adhesion and separation.
Pay attention to the word 'سهولة' (ease) which is key.
2
Analyze the alternative definition
The passage later defines moisture as ease of receiving shapes and leaving them, based on water's two attributes. See how this addresses further objections.
This definition tries to capture the nature of water more comprehensively.
3
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator notes that the author's inclusion of Ibn Sina's objection is an oversight, as it was originally directed at a different definition. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( قال ابن سينا إلخ) في الشفاء ما حاصله أنَّ بعض الأجسام الرطبة إذا فتشنا أحواله نجد فيه التصاقا بما يماسه فالجمهور ظنوا أن الرطوبة هي الالتصاق وليس كذلك. وإلا لكان ما أشد التصاقاً أرطب. فيلزم أن يكون الدهن والعسل أرطب من الماء. قال الإمام : هذا إنما يلزم لو فسر الرطوبة بنفس الالتصاق لكنها عبارة عن سهولة الالتصاق بالغير مع سهولة الانفصال عنه ولا شك أن الماء أكمل في هذا المعنى وبما نقلناه ظهر لك أنَّ اعتراض ابن سينا على من جعل الرطوبة نفس الالتصاق وإن تغيير الإمام تفسير الجمهور إلى تفسيره لدفع الاعتراض المذكور فإيراد المصنف اعتراض ابن سينا على تفسير الإمام والجواب عنه بما ذكره سهو قوله : (لأنه إذا كان إلخ) التقريب غير تام لأنه لم يجعل الالتصاق معلولاً للرطوبة بل سهولته. قوله : (أي كيفية تقتضي إلخ) فسر سهولة الالتصاق بهذا لأنَّ السهولة أمر نسبي وليس من مقولة الكيف وقد يعترض على اعتبار سهولة الالتصاق بأنه يوجب أن يكون اليابس المدقوق جداً كالعظام المحرقة رطباً لكونها كذلك. ويجاب بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لمخالطة الأجزاء الهوائية. وهذا إنما يتم على رأي من يقول برطوبة الهواء وسهولة التصاقه لولا مانع فرط اللطافة لا على رأي الإمام .

من شك أن كون العسل والدهن أرطب من الماء باطل ) فهي سهولة ) أي الرطوبة كيفية تقتضي سهولة (قبول الأشكال و سهولة (تركها) وذلك لأن الماء له وصفان ( أحدهما ما يقتضي سهولة الالتصاق والانفصال والثاني ما يقتضي سهولة قبول الأشكال وتركها ولا شبهة في أنَّ الماء يوصف بأنه رطب باعتبار أحد هذين الوصفين . فإذا بطل الأول تعين الثاني (قلنا : هو ) أي العسل (أدوم التصاقاً) وأشد التصاقاً الماء ( لا أسهل ) التصاقا منه ونحن لم نفسر الرطوبة بنفس الالتصاق حتى يلزم أن يكون ما هو أشد وأقوى في الالتصاق أرطب ولا بدوام الالتصاق حتى يكون الأدوم أكثر رطوبة بل بسهولة الالتصاق فاللازم منه أن يكون الأسهل التصاقاً أرطب وليس العسل أو الدهن أسهل التصاقاً من الماء بل بالعكس وأيضاً قد اعتبر في الرطوبة الانفصال وليس العسل أشد انفصالاً من الماء فلا يلزم كونه أرطب ويرد ذلك الاعتراض أيضاً (في تفسيرها بسهولة قبول الأشكال لأنه إذا كان تشكل الجسم

قوله : (له وصفان وجود الوصفين غير معلوم إنما المعلوم سهولة الالتصاق والانفصال وسهولة قبول الأشكال وتركها .

قوله : ( باعتبار أحد هذين الوصفين فيه بحث لجواز أن يكون وصفه باعتبار البلة التي في طبيعته كما هو متفاهم العوام .
اتحاد .
قوله : ( تعين الثاني) فصح التفريع المستفاد من الفاء في قوله فهي سهولة إلخ . قوله : ( وأيضاً إلخ) مبنى هذا اعتبار الانفصال وما سيجيء في المتن اعتبار سهولته فلا

قوله : ( ولا شبهة في أن الماء إلخ قد يمنع ذلك بجواز أن تكون رطوبته باعتبار أمر آخر مجهول الماهية.

قوله : (قلنا : هو أدوم (التصاقاً اعترض عليه بأن المذكور في كلام بعض المتقدمين أنَّ الجسم إنما يكون رطباً إذا كان بحيث يلتصق بما يلامسه فنقله ابن سينا ورده بما ذكره المصنف نقلاً عنه فلا يستقيم حينئذ جواب المصنف والشارح، لأن مبنى اعتراض الشيخ على أنه لا تعرض في كلامهم للانفصال أصلاً ولا للسهولة في جانب الالتصاق حتى يكون مبنى الجواب أن سهولة الالتصاق تستلزم سهولة الانفصال، على أنَّ الاستلزام ممنوع. نعم قد يجاب عما ذكره الشيخ بأن المقصود تفسير الرطوبة التي في البسيط وحال الرطوبات المحسوسة الأخر يعلم بالمقايسة إذ كلما كان أجزاء الرطب الحقيقي أكثر من الأجزاء الأخر كان الجسم أرطب وكون العسل أشد التصاقاً منه وكذا الدهن ليس بمتعين.

قوله: (وليس العسل أو الدهن أسهل التصاقاً من الماء إلخ لاحتياج التصاقهما إلى زيادة اعتمال بخلاف التصاق الماء .
His saying: (Ibn Sina said, etc.) In al-Shifa, its summary is that some moist bodies, when we examine their states, we find in them adhesion to what they contact. So the majority thought that moisture is adhesion, but it is not so. Otherwise, whatever is more adhesive would be more moist. So it would follow that oil and honey are more moist than water. Imam al-Razi said: This only follows if moisture is interpreted as adhesion itself, but it is an expression for ease of adhesion to another along with ease of separation from it, and there is no doubt that water is more complete in this meaning. And by what we have transmitted, it became clear to you that Ibn Sina's objection is against those who made moisture itself adhesion, and the Imam's changing the interpretation of the majority to his interpretation is to repel the mentioned objection. So the author's bringing Ibn Sina's objection against the Imam's interpretation and the answer to it by what he mentioned is an oversight. His saying: (Because if it is, etc.) The approximation is incomplete because he did not make adhesion an effect of moisture, but rather its ease. His saying: (i.e., a quality that necessitates, etc.) He interpreted ease of adhesion by this because ease is a relative matter and not from the category of quality (kayf). And it may be objected to considering ease of adhesion that it necessitates that a very finely ground dry substance like burnt bones would be moist because it is likewise. And it is answered that it is possible that this is due to mixture with airy parts. And this only works on the opinion of one who affirms the moisture of air and the ease of its adhesion were it not for the obstacle of extreme subtlety, not on the opinion of the Imam.

[line] From the doubt that honey and oil being more moist than water is false: so it is ease – i.e., moisture is a quality that necessitates ease of receiving shapes and ease of leaving them. This is because water has two attributes: one that necessitates ease of adhesion and separation, and the second that necessitates ease of receiving shapes and leaving them. And there is no doubt that water is described as moist by virtue of one of these two attributes. So if the first is invalidated, the second is determined. We say: honey is more lasting in adhesion and more intense in adhesion than water, but not easier in adhesion than it. And we did not interpret moisture as adhesion itself such that whatever is more intense and stronger in adhesion would be more moist, nor by permanence of adhesion such that the more permanent would be more moist, but by ease of adhesion. So what follows is that whatever is easier in adhesion is more moist, and honey or oil are not easier in adhesion than water, but rather the opposite. Also, separation has been considered in moisture, and honey is not more intensely separateable than water, so it does not follow that it is more moist. And that objection also applies to the interpretation of moisture by ease of receiving shapes, because when the body is shaped...

His saying: (He has two attributes) The existence of the two attributes is not known; only ease of adhesion and separation and ease of receiving shapes and leaving them are known.

His saying: (By virtue of one of these two attributes) In it is a discussion because it is possible that its description is by virtue of the wetness (bulla) in its nature, as is commonly understood.

[line] Union.

His saying: (The second is determined) So the inference derived from the fa in his saying 'so it is ease etc.' is sound. His saying: (And also, etc.) Its basis is the consideration of separation, and what will come in the text is the consideration of its ease, so there is no union.

His saying: (And there is no doubt that water, etc.) It may be prevented by the possibility that its moisture is by virtue of another matter of unknown essence.

His saying: (We say: it is more lasting in adhesion) It was objected to it that what is mentioned in the speech of some earlier scholars is that a body is only moist if it is such that it adheres to what it contacts. Ibn Sina transmitted this and refuted it by what the author transmitted from him. So then the answer of the author and the commentator is not sound, because the basis of the objection of the shaykh is that there is no mention in their speech of separation at all, nor of ease in the aspect of adhesion, such that the basis of the answer would be that ease of adhesion entails ease of separation, and that entailment is denied. Yes, it may be answered regarding what the shaykh mentioned that the intent is the interpretation of moisture in the simple (al-basit) and the state of other perceived moistures is known by comparison, for whenever the parts of the real moisture are more than the other parts, the body is more moist. And that honey and oil are more adhesive than it is not certain.

His saying: (And honey or oil are not easier in adhesion than water, etc.) Because their adhesion requires more effort, unlike the adhesion of water.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's objection to the definition of moisture as ease of adhesion and separation. Ibn Sina argues from his work al-Shifa that if moisture were ease of adhesion, then more adhesive substances like honey and oil would be more moist than water, which is false. The response clarifies that the definition includes both ease of adhesion and ease of separation, and water is more complete in both. The commentator notes that Ibn Sina's objection actually targets those who define moisture as adhesion itself, not the nuanced definition. The passage then introduces an alternative definition: moisture is ease of receiving shapes and leaving them, based on water's two attributes. The response to the objection reiterates that honey is more adhesive but not easier to adhere or separate. The commentary further discusses potential objections, emphasizing that ease of adhesion and separation are relative and not qualities. It also addresses an objection that if ease of adhesion were the definition, then finely ground dry substances would be moist, but answers that this relies on affirming the moisture of air, which the Imam does not. The passage concludes with additional commentary on the necessity of considering separation and ease.

سهولة الالتصاق

Misconception: If something is more adhesive, it is more moist.
Correction: Moisture is defined by ease of adhesion and separation, not by intensity. Water is less adhesive but easier to adhere and separate than honey or oil.

Checkpoint
Why does the response argue that honey is not more moist than water?

Key Takeaway
Moisture is defined by ease of adhesion and separation, not by intensity of adhesion, and water is the standard for this ease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken intensity for ease in your own spiritual life? That is, do you think that being more forceful or rigid in your worship makes you more devout, when in fact true devotion is about ease and flexibility? How can you apply the principle of this lesson—that moisture is ease, not intensity—to your relationship with Allah? What would it mean to have ease in adhering to His commands and ease in separating from sins?
?

What would it take to test them against a more precise understanding, even if it means redefining something you thought you knew? Will you take a step today to question one such assumption in your faith?

Refutation of Ibn Sina's Definition of Wetness
Refutes Ibn Sina's definition of wetness as ease of shaping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina's definition of wetness as ease of shaping is invalid and why ease of separation is a necessary criterion.

Core Idea
Wetness is not just shaping; separation ease is equally required.

Ibn Sina defined wetness as ease of taking on and leaving shapes. This passage challenges that definition by showing it leads to absurd consequences—namely that air would be wetter than water. It also introduces the concept of ease of separation as a necessary component of true wetness. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Examine the initial objection about shape duration
Read the first part of the passage where it argues that if wetness is ease of shaping, then something with a more permanent shape would be wetter. Note the challenge: 'what is your answer is our answer.'
2
Understand the honey counterexample
The passage uses honey: it is easy to shape (adhere) but hard to separate. This shows that ease of shaping alone is insufficient; ease of separation is also essential. See how this undermines Ibn Sina.
Honey is sticky but not necessarily wetter than water.
3
Grasp the conclusive refutation via air
The passage directly states that Ibn Sina's definition makes air wetter than water, which is absurd because air is not wet. This is a reductio ad absurdum. Learn how this invalidates the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالأشكال الـ [الأدوم؟] الغريبة لأجل رطوبته لزم أن يكون ما هو أدوم شكلاً أرطب وليس كذلك إذ شكلاً أيبس فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا. وأيضاً فسهولة الانفصال معتبرة في حقيقتها والعسل وإن فرضنا أنه سهل اتصاله حتى زاد في سهولة الاتصال على الماء لكن يعسر انفصاله فعلى تقدير كون العسل أسهل التصاقاً من الماء لا يلزم أيضاً كونه أرطب إذ ليس أسهل انفصالاً منه. ثم نقول يبطل تفسير ابن سينا للرطوبة بسهولة التشكل وتركه أنه يوجب أن يكون الهواء رطباً بل أن يكون أرطب من الماء لأنه أرق قواماً منه وأقبل للتشكلات الغريبة وتركها بسهولة.
By the most [permanent?] strange shapes, for the sake of its wetness, it would follow that what is more lasting in shape is wetter, but it is not so, since a shape is drier, so what is your answer is our answer. Also, ease of separation is considered in its reality, and honey, if we suppose that it is easy to connect, to the point that it increased in ease of connection over water, but its separation is difficult, so on the assumption that honey is easier to adhere than water, it does not also follow that it is wetter, since it is not easier to separate. Then we say: The interpretation of Ibn Sina of wetness as ease of forming shapes and leaving them is invalidated, because it makes air wet, rather it makes it wetter than water, because it is thinner in consistency than it and more receptive to strange shapes and leaving them easily.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage refutes Ibn Sina's definition of wetness. It begins by presenting an objection: if wetness is defined by ease of shaping, then something that holds a shape longer would be wetter, but that is false. The response given is that ease of separation is also a criterion. The example of honey is used: honey is easy to shape but hard to separate, so it is not wetter than water. Then the passage directly refutes Ibn Sina's definition by showing it entails that air is wetter than water, since air is thinner and more receptive to shapes. The refutation is that this consequence is absurd, so the definition is invalid.

Correction: Ease of separation is also necessary; otherwise honey and air would be considered wetter than water.

The Agreement on Mixing Wet and Dry: Defining Wetness as a Quality
Clarifies the agreement on mixing wet and dry and distinguishes moisture from true wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the agreement about mixing wet and dry to achieve cohesion applies specifically to water and not to every wet thing, and that true wetness is a quality permeating a body, not just surface moisture.

Core Idea
Wetness is a quality that coheres, not just surface moisture.

The commentator clarifies a crucial distinction: the agreement that mixing wet and dry gives cohesion refers specifically to water, not any wet substance. Moreover, he distinguishes between mere moisture (بلة) and true wetness (رطوبة) as a quality that permeates the body. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the commentator's specification that 'wet' here means water, not everything wet.
2
Identify the agreement's scope
The agreement is about mixing water and earth. See how the commentator limits it.
3
Understand the distinction between moisture and wetness
The passage presents two possible meanings of 'moisture' and clarifies that only the quality of wetness yields the ruling.
4
Apply the distinction
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it prevents misunderstanding the nature of wetness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (واتفقوا على أن خلط إلخ) الاتفاق إنما هو على أنَّ خلط الرطب الذي هو الماء لا كل رطب في الشفاء في فصل انفعالات العناصر يستمسك جوهر الماء بعد سيلانه بمخالطة الأرض ويستمسك جوهر الأرض عن تشتته بمخالطة الماء وقيل: إِنَّ ذلك الحكم إنما هو للرطب بمعنى ذي البلة فإنَّ إطلاق البلة شائع وفيه أنه إن أراد بالبلة ما سيجيء من الجسم الرطب الجاري على ظاهر جسم آخر فلا شك أنَّ خلط المبتل باليابس لا يفيد الحكم المذكور وإن أراد الكيفية السارية في الجسم المحسوسة فهي الرطوبة.
His saying: ('And they agreed that mixing etc.') The agreement is that mixing wet which is water, not every wet thing, in the Shifa in the chapter on the affections of the elements, the substance of water after its flow is held together by mixing with earth, and the substance of earth is held from its dispersion by mixing with water. And it was said: That ruling is only for wet in the sense of possessing moisture, for the term moisture is common. And in it is that if he means by moisture what will come of the wet body flowing on the surface of another body, then there is no doubt that mixing the moist with the dry does not produce the mentioned ruling; and if he means the quality permeating the body that is sensed, then it is wetness.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage explains the agreement mentioned in the text. The commentator specifies that the agreement refers to mixing water (not any wet thing) with earth, which gives cohesion to both. Then a distinction is introduced: the ruling might apply only to wetness in the sense of having moisture (بلة), which is a common term. The commentator then clarifies that if 'moisture' means the flowing on the surface, then mixing such a moist body with a dry one does not give cohesion. But if it means the quality that permeates the body and is sensed, that is true wetness (رطوبة). Thus the passage clarifies the proper definition of wetness as a quality, not just moisture.

Misconception: People often think that wetness and moisture are the same, so any wet substance when mixed with a dry one will cause cohesion.
Correction: The passage shows that the agreement applies only to water, and true wetness is a quality that permeates a body, not just surface moisture. Mixing a merely moist surface with a dry substance does not produce cohesion.

?

What would it take for you to shift from surface moisture to true wetness in your worship?
?

?

What is causing the lack of cohesion—superficiality, distraction, or something else? Identify one area today where you can make your practice permeate like true wetness.

The Objection of Fire Being Wetter Than Water and Its Answer
Objection that fire is wetter than water by definition and its refutation through distinguishing simple fire and refining the concept of wetness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of wetness as ease of shaping leads to an objection about fire, and how the answer distinguishes simple fire and clarifies the relationship between thinness and wetness.

Core Idea
Definitions must be carefully refined to avoid absurd consequences.

This passage presents a sharp objection: if wetness is defined as ease of accepting shapes, then fire, being thinner than water, would be wetter than water. The answer requires careful distinctions about what kind of fire is meant and what wetness really is. This lesson walks through the objection, the answer, and the deeper clarification that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
Notice the objection starts with 'ربما لزموا'.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection claims that fire must be wetter than water because it is thinner in consistency (أرق قواماً) and therefore easier to shape. Understand the logic: thinness implies ease of shaping, which is the definition of wetness.
The objector uses the definition of wetness given earlier.
3
Understand the distinction between simple and composite fire
The answer denies that simple (pure) fire is easy to shape. Ordinary fire is mixed with air, so its ease comes from the mixture, not from the fire itself.
This distinction blocks the objection for ordinary fire.
4
Grasp the refutation that thinness alone is not sufficient
The passage argues that thinness does not necessarily cause ease of shaping; otherwise wetness would be identical to thinness. Wetness accompanies thinness, softness, and fluidity but is none of them.
This refutes the underlying assumption linking thinness and ease.
5
Examine the additional responses about air and quality vs. effect
The passage also addresses the implication that air is wetter than water, distinguishing between sensed moisture and the quality of wetness. Finally, it notes that the quality itself is not necessarily increased; the effect may depend on the recipient.
These responses complete the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ربما لزموا أن النار يابسة عندكم وهذا التعريف الذي ذكرتموه للرطوبة يوجب كونها أرطب من الماء لأنها أرق قواماً من الماء والهواء أيضاً فتكون أسهل قبولاً للأشكال وتركها منهما والجواب منع ذلك في النار البسيطة أي لا نسلم أن النار الصرفة البسيطة أسهل قبولاً للأشكال من الماء وأن رقة القوام وحدها كافية في سهولة التشكل حتى يلزم أن يكون الأرق أسهل قبولاً وما عندنا من النار ليس بسيطاً بل هو مركب من الهواء ومختلط به فجاز أن يكون سهولة قبوله للأشكال وتركها بسبب اختلاط الهواء فلا يلزم. قوله: ( لأنها أرق قواماً هذا التعليل يفيد بأن رقة القوام تقتضي سهولة قبول الأشكال وهو باطل وإلا لكانت الرطوبة عبارة عن رقة القوام نعم إنها تجامع رقة القوام واللين والسيلان وليست شيئاً منها. وبما ذكرنا ظهر الجواب عما ذكر من لزوم كون الهواء أرطب من الماء لأنه أرق قواماً منه. المحسوسات إنما هي البلة لا ما اعتبر فيه سهولة قبول أشكال حادثة لأن الهواء رطب بهذا المعنى ولا يحس فيه رطوبة. بقي هاهنا بحث وهو أن لزوم كون الهواء أرطب من الماء لم يندفع بشيء مما ذكرناه مع أنه باطل قطعاً، ويمكن أن يجاب عنه أيضاً : بأنَّ الرطوبة الكيفية المقتضية للسهولة المذكورة لا نفسها وكون الكيفية المذكورة في الهواء أزيد مما في الماء ممنوع، وزيادة الأثر لا تدل على زيادة المؤثر لجواز أن يكون بحسب القابل، وجرم الهواء لكونه أرق قواماً من الماء أقبل للسهولة المذكورة. وبهذا التحقيق يظهر اندفاع ما سيورده من لزوم كون النار أرطب من الماء والهواء لكونها أسهل قبولاً للأشكال منهما.
They might insist that fire is dry according to you, and this definition you mentioned of wetness makes it wetter than water because it is thinner in consistency than water and air as well, so it would be easier in accepting shapes and leaving them than both. The answer is to deny that in simple fire, i.e., we do not grant that pure simple fire is easier in accepting shapes than water, and that thinness of consistency alone is sufficient for ease of shaping such that the thinner is easier in accepting. And what we have of fire is not simple, but rather it is composed of air and mixed with it, so it is possible that its ease of accepting shapes and leaving them is due to the mixture of air, so it does not follow. His saying: ('because it is thinner in consistency') this explanation suggests that thinness of consistency necessitates ease of accepting shapes, and that is false, otherwise wetness would be an expression of thinness of consistency. Indeed, it accompanies thinness, softness, and fluidity, and is none of them. And by what we have mentioned, the answer appears to what was mentioned of the implication that air is wetter than water because it is thinner in consistency. What is sensed is moisture, not what involves ease of accepting transient shapes, because air is wet in this sense but moisture is not sensed in it. Here remains a discussion: the implication that air is wetter than water is not repelled by anything we mentioned, even though it is absolutely false. And it can also be answered: wetness is the quality that necessitates the mentioned ease, not itself, and that the mentioned quality in air is more than in water is denied, and the increase of effect does not indicate increase of the cause because it may be according to the recipient, and the substance of air because it is thinner is more receptive to the mentioned ease. And by this investigation, the repulsion of what he will mention of the implication that fire is wetter than water and air because it is easier in accepting shapes appears.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: if wetness is defined by ease of shaping, then fire, being thinner, would be wetter than water. The answer distinguishes between simple and composite fire. Simple fire is not easily shaped; only composite fire is, due to air. Then the passage refutes the notion that thinness alone causes ease of shaping, and explains that wetness is a quality that accompanies thinness but is not identical to it. It also discusses that sensed moisture is different from the quality of wetness. Finally, it gives an additional response: the quality of wetness itself is not increased by thinness; rather, the effect may be due to the recipient. Thus the objection is repelled.

Key Takeaway
The objection that fire is wetter than water using the ease-of-shaping definition is refuted by distinguishing simple fire, denying that thinness alone causes ease, and clarifying that wetness is a quality causing ease, not identical to thinness.

?

?

?

Critique of Ṭūsī's Contradiction on Determinant of Speed
Critique of Ṭūsī's contradiction on determinant of speed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ṭūsī contradicted himself regarding the determinant of speed and the proposed resolution.

Core Idea
Ṭūsī undermined his own argument by claiming inclination alone determines speed.

This passage examines an internal inconsistency in al-Ṭūsī's physical theory. While the current investigation draws from Ṭūsī, his own commentary on Ibn Sīnā suggests that inclination suffices to determine speed, contradicting the necessity of an external resistance. The commentator presents Ṭūsī's words and then offers a possible reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the quote from Ṭūsī's Sharḥ al-Ishārāt.
2
Identify Ṭūsī's claim
Identify what Ṭūsī claims in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt about the determinant of speed and slowness.
He says it is inclination, not external resistance.
3
Understand the contradiction
Realize why this claim contradicts the need for a resistance (al-muʿāwiq) that determines speed.
If inclination alone suffices, resistance is redundant.
4
Consider the proposed resolution
Examine the commentator's suggestion that the degrees of inclination still involve plenum in the distance, so resistance is needed.
This attempt to reconcile is left for reflection.
5
Reflect on the unresolved tension
Think about whether the resolution fully resolves the contradiction or if Ṭūsī's position remains problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي هاهنا بحث وهو أن هذا التحقيق الذي أورده الشارح منقول عن الطوسي وقد ناقض نفسه حيث دل كلامه في شرح الإشارات على أن محدد مراتب السرعة والبطء لا يلزم أن يكون معاوقاً لجواز أن يكون هو الميل قال في ذلك الشرح : الحركة لا تنفك عن حد ما من السرعة والبطء، ولما كانت الطبيعة التي هي مبدأ الحركة شيئاً لا يقبل الشدة والضعف كانت جميع الحركات المختلفة بالشدة والضعف يعني السرعة والبطء إليها واحدة وكانت صدور حركة معينة منها ممتنعة لعدم الأولوية، فاقتضت أولاً أمراً يشتد ويضعف بحسب اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة من الكم أعني الكبر والصغر أو الكيف أعني التكاثف والتخلخل والوضع أعني اندماج الأجزاء وانتفاشها أو غير ذلك وبحسب ما يخرج عنه كحال ما فيه أي من رقة القوام وغلظة وذلك الأمر هو الميل هذا كلامه طويناه على غره ولا حاجة بنا هاهنا إلى إبطاله أو تصحيحه وهو صريح في أن ما يحدد حال الحركة في السرعة والبطء هو الميل، اللهم إلا أن يقال : مراتب الميل وإن كانت تحدد مراتب الحركة إلا أن في تعيين مراتبه دخلاً لما في المسافة من الملأ البتة فيثبت الاحتياج إلى المعاوق ويندفع التناقض فليتأمل.
There remains here a discussion: that this investigation which the commentator presented is transmitted from al-Ṭūsī, and he contradicted himself because his words in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt indicate that the determinant of the degrees of speed and slowness does not have to be a resistance, because it could be inclination. He said in that commentary: Motion is inseparable from a certain limit of speed and slowness. And since nature, which is the principle of motion, is something that does not accept intensity and weakness, all motions differing in intensity and weakness – meaning speed and slowness – towards it are one, and the occurrence of a particular motion from it is impossible due to lack of priority. So it first necessitated something that intensifies and weakens according to the difference of the body having nature in quantity – I mean bigness and smallness – or quality – I mean density and rarity – or position – I mean interlocking and spreading of parts – or other than that, and according to what comes out of it, like the state of what is in it, i.e., fineness and coarseness of texture, and that thing is inclination. This is his speech, we have summarized it, and we do not need here to refute or correct it. It is explicit that what determines the state of motion in speed and slowness is inclination. However, it might be said: although the degrees of inclination determine the degrees of motion, yet in determining its degrees there is an input because of what is in the distance from plenum, so the need for resistance is established and the contradiction is repelled. So let this be considered.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a critique of al-Ṭūsī's inconsistency. The commentator notes that the current investigation is taken from Ṭūsī, but Ṭūsī contradicted himself in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt. There, Ṭūsī argued that the determinant of speed and slowness is inclination itself, not an external resistance. He reasoned that nature alone cannot produce varying speeds because it is uniform, so an additional factor (inclination) that varies with size, density, etc. determines the speed. This implies that inclination alone determines speed, contradicting the need for an external resistance. The commentator then suggests a possible resolution: although inclination determines the degrees of motion, the distance (in void) has plenum involved, so resistance is still needed. He leaves the matter for reflection.

Key Terms
الطوسي (al-Ṭūsī)
محدد مراتب السرعة (determinant of degrees of speed)
المعاوق (resistance)
الميل (inclination)
شرح الإشارات (Sharḥ al-Ishārāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ṭūsī's position on speed determination is consistent.
Correction: In fact, Ṭūsī contradicted himself because in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt he argued that inclination alone determines speed, implying no need for external resistance, whereas his other works suggest the need for resistance.

Checkpoint
What does Tusi claim in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt that contradicts the need for external resistance?
A) That nature alone determines speed.
B) That inclination alone determines speed.
C) That speed is determined by the size of the body.
D) That resistance is the only determinant.

Key Takeaway
Ṭūsī's own words in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt undermine the necessity of an external resistance, creating a contradiction in his physical theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a position on an important matter, do you ever examine whether your own arguments contradict one another? What would it take for you to be as honest in self-critique as the commentator was with Ṭūsī?
?
Consider a belief you hold strongly about yourself—perhaps about your abilities or character. Could it be that you have ignored evidence that contradicts that belief? How might you uncover and face such a contradiction?

The Ancient Denial of Real Color
The commentator presents the ancient denial of real color existence and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient argument that color is not a real entity but an effect of light interacting with small transparent particles.

Core Idea
Color is not a real quality — it is an illusion caused by light and air.

This lesson explores a radical ancient theory that challenges our everyday assumption that objects have real colors. The commentator presents the view that colors are merely imagined, and provides examples to illustrate how whiteness arises from mechanical interactions of light and small transparent particles. We'll examine the examples and the reasoning behind this denial of color's independent existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how the author states the denial of color and then gives examples.
The passage opens with a structural introduction before the ancient view.
2
Identify the core claim and examples
Find the claim that color has no existence. Then list the three examples: foam, snow, and powdered crystal/glass. Observe how each example demonstrates that whiteness appears without a real white substance.
The examples share the feature of tiny transparent particles mixed with light.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Think about why the ancients used these examples. Ask: If whiteness is not real, what causes the perception? The passage says it is due to the mixture of luminous air with small transparent particles.
This challenges common sense and introduces a key theme in philosophical debates about perception.

Source Text

القسم الأول: في الألوان. قدمها على الأضواء مع كونها مشروطة بها إما في رؤيتها أو وجودها على ما سيأتي لأنها أكثر وجوداً في الأجسام التي عندنا. وفيه مقاصد ثلاثة: المقصد الأول: قول القدماء في اللون. قال بعض من القدماء: لا وجود للون أصلا بل كلها متخيلة وإنما يتخيل البياض من مخالطة الهواء المضيء للأجزاء الشفافة المتصغرة جداً كما في زبد الماء فإنه أبيض ولا سبب البياضه سوى ما ذكر وكما في الثلج فإنه أجزاء جمدية صغار شفافة خالطها الهواء ونفذ فيها الضوء فيتخيل أنَّ هناك بياضاً وكما في البلور والزجاج المسحوقين سحقاً ناعماً فإنه يرى فيهما بياض مع أنَّ أجزاءهما المتصغرة لم ينفعل بعضها عن بعض عند الاجتماع حتى يحدث فيهما اللون وفي موضع الشق من الزجاج وفي بعض النسخ من الشفاف الثخين فإنه يرى. قوله: (ولما كانت إلخ الأظهر ولما كان كل واحد منهما مرئياً بالذات إذ لا دخل في جعل مباحثهما قسمين للاشتراط المذكور. قوله: (أورد كلا منهما إلخ) أي تنبيهاً على تغايرهما باعتبار الشرطية والمشروطية. قوله: (مع كونها مشروطة بها والشرط مقدم على المشروط بالطبع. قوله: (لأنها أكثر إلخ) فإنَّ ما سوى الهواء كلها ملونة والمضيء منها ليست إلا النار وكون اللون مشروطاً في وجوده أو ظهوره بالضوء لا ينافي عمومه كما لا يخفى. قوله: (لم ينفعل بعضها عن بعض) لعدم الرطوبة الموجبة لتلاقي سطوح الأجزاء المتصغرة الموجب للتفاعل بخلاف الثلج.
First Section: On Colors. He presented them before lights even though they are conditioned by them either in vision or existence as will come, because they are more abundant in existence in the bodies that are with us. In it are three aims: The First Aim: The statement of the ancients about color. Some of the ancients said: There is no existence for color at all, rather they are all imagined. Whiteness is only imagined due to the mixture of luminous air with very small transparent particles, as in the foam of water, for it is white, and there is no cause for its whiteness except what was mentioned; and as in snow, for it is small transparent ice particles mixed with air and light penetrated in them, so it is imagined that there is whiteness; and as in crystal and glass powdered finely, for whiteness is seen in them even though their small particles did not affect each other upon meeting so as to produce color; and at the place of the crack in glass, and in some copies of thick transparent, for it is seen. His statement: 'When they were etc.' The more apparent: since each one of them is visible by itself, for there is no involvement in making their investigations two sections due to the mentioned conditionality. His statement: 'He presented each one of them etc.' i.e. alerting to their difference in terms of condition and conditioned. His statement: 'even though they are conditioned by them' and the condition is prior to the conditioned by nature. His statement: 'because they are more etc.' For everything other than air is colored, and the luminous among them is only fire. And the fact that color is conditioned in its existence or appearance by light does not contradict its generality, as is not hidden. His statement: 'did not affect each other' due to the lack of moisture that causes the surfaces of the small particles to meet, causing interaction, unlike snow.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage begins the first section on colors. It explains why colors are discussed before lights: they are more abundant in objects around us. Then it presents the first aim about the ancients' view on color. Some ancients denied the real existence of color, claiming it is merely imagined. The cause of this imagination is the mixture of luminous air with very small transparent particles. Examples are given: whiteness in foam, snow, powdered crystal/glass, and at the crack of thick glass. In each case, the whiteness is not a real color but an effect of light interacting with tiny transparent particles. The commentator clarifies the rationale for presenting colors first, the conditionality of color on light, and why the examples work: the particles do not interact because they are dry and lack moisture, unlike snow which has moisture and therefore interacts. This view challenges the common assumption that color is a real quality inherent in objects.

Correction: The ancients argue that color is not real; it is an effect of light and air on transparent particles.

?

?

The Ancients' View: Whiteness as an Imaginary Perception
Al-Qudama' deny real existence of color and explain whiteness as imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient philosophical view that colors, especially whiteness, are not real qualities but imaginary perceptions caused by light and transparent particles.

Core Idea
Colors are not real; whiteness is an effect of light mixing with tiny transparent particles.

Some ancient philosophers denied that colors exist as real qualities in objects. Instead, they argued that our perception of colors arises from physical interactions between light and transparent particles. This passage explains their view, focusing on whiteness and providing several examples to support the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage and the English translation to get an overview of the argument and the examples.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'luminous air' and 'transparent particles'.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement that all colors are imaginary and that whiteness is specifically explained by the mixture of luminous air with transparent particles.
3
Analyze the supporting examples
Consider each example (foam, snow, crushed glass, crack in glass) and how it demonstrates the claim that whiteness appears without an inherent color.
Notice that the examples involve transparent materials that appear white when broken into small pieces or mixed with air.

المقصد الأول : قول القدماء في اللون قال بعض من القدماء: لا وجود للون أصلا بل كلها متخيلة وإنما يتخيل البياض من مخالطة الهواء المضيء للأجزاء الشفافة المتصغرة جداً كما في زبد الماء فإنه أبيض ولا سبب البياض سوى ما ذكر وكما في الثلج فإنه أجزاء جمدية صغار شفافة خالطها الهواء ونفذ فيها الضوء فيتخيل أنَّ هناك بياضاً وكما في البلور والزجاج المسحوقين سحقاً ناعماً فإنه يرى فيهما بياض مع أنَّ أجزاءهما المتصغرة لم ينفعل بعضها عن بعض عند الاجتماع حتى يحدث فيهما اللون وفي موضع الشق من الزجاج وفي بعض النسخ من الشفاف الثخين فإنه يرى ذلك الموضع أبيض مع كونه أبعد من حدوث البياض فيه وقد مر هذه الأمثلة في صدر الكتاب.
The First Aim: The Saying of the Ancients on Color. Some of the ancients said: There is no existence for color at all, rather all colors are imaginary. Whiteness is imagined from the mixture of luminous air with very small transparent particles, as in foam of water – it is white and there is no cause for its whiteness except what was mentioned – and as in snow – it consists of small transparent icy particles mixed with air, and light penetrates them, so an observer imagines that there is whiteness – and as in crushed crystal and glass, when ground finely, one sees whiteness in them despite the fact that their small parts have not reacted upon each other upon combination so as to produce a color in them – and in the place of a crack in glass, and in some copies 'of thick transparent material', one sees that place white despite its being further from the occurrence of whiteness in it. And these examples have already been mentioned at the beginning of the book.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof by example. It presents the view of some ancient philosophers who deny the real existence of color, considering all colors to be imaginary perceptions. The author then focuses on whiteness to illustrate this claim. The ancients explain that whiteness is not a real quality but results from the combination of luminous air and very small transparent particles. Several examples are given: foam (which is white due to air mixing with water particles), snow (where small transparent ice crystals mixed with air and light create the perception of whiteness), crushed crystal and glass (where fine particles create whiteness without any chemical reaction), and a crack in thick glass (which appears white even though it is distant from where whiteness would normally occur). The author notes that these examples have been mentioned earlier in the book.

How the Ancients Explained Blackness as Imaginary
Al-Qudama' explain how blackness is imagined by opposite and by water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient explanation of blackness as an imagined quality rather than a real color, and the two mechanisms they proposed: absence of light/air and the role of water.

Core Idea
Blackness, for the ancients, is not a real color but a visual illusion caused by darkness.

In this passage, al-Qudama' (the ancients) present their view that blackness is not a real property but an imagined one. They offer two related explanations: one based on the absence of light and air, and another attributing it to the effect of water. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and examines the supporting example of wet clothes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two main explanations and the concluding example.
2
Identify the two explanations
The passage presents two mechanisms for the imagination of blackness. Find where each is introduced and how they relate to each other.
The first is the opposite of whiteness; the second involves water.
3
Understand the water mechanism
Examine how water is said to cause blackness: it expels air from the body and blocks light penetration due to lower transparency.
The key is that water's transparency is less than air's.
4
Consider the example of wet clothes
The passage gives everyday evidence: wet clothes become darker. Reflect on how this supports the water-based explanation.
This example makes the argument concrete.
5
Reflect on the imaginary nature
Ask yourself why the ancients considered blackness imaginary rather than real. What does this tell us about their theory of perception?
This connects to broader themes in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: والسواد يتخيل بضد ذلك وهو عدم غور الهواء والضوء في عمق الجسم ومنهم من قال: الماء يوجب السواد أي يوجب تخيله لما يخرج الهواء أن الماء إذا وصل إلى الجسم ونفذ في أعماقه أخرج منها الهواء وليس إشفافه كإشفاف الهواء حتى ينفذ الضوء إلى السطوح فتبقى السطوح مظلمة فيتخيل أن هناك سواداً. وأيضاً فإن الثياب إذا ابتلت مالت إلى السواد فدل ذلك على أن الماء يوجب تخيل السواد.
They said: And blackness is imagined by the opposite of that, namely the absence of the penetration of air and light into the depth of the body. And among them is he who said: Water causes blackness, meaning it causes its imagination, because when water reaches the body and penetrates its depths, it expels the air from them, and its transparency is not like the transparency of air so that light penetrates to the surfaces, so the surfaces remain dark, thus one imagines that there is blackness. Also, when clothes become wet, they tend toward blackness, and that indicates that water causes the imagination of blackness.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the ancient explanation for the perception of blackness. It first states that blackness is imagined by the opposite of whiteness: the absence of air and light penetrating the body. This leaves the surface dark, creating the imagination of blackness. Then it reports a specific view: some ancients attribute blackness to water. The mechanism is that water, being less transparent than air, expels air from the body's depths and prevents light from reaching the surfaces, resulting in darkness. A supporting example is wet clothes, which become darker, demonstrating that water causes the imagination of blackness.

Correction: The passage shows they considered blackness an imaginary quality, caused by absence of light/air or by water.

Checkpoint

A) The inherent blackness of objects
B) The absence of light
C) The presence of water expelling air from the body
D) The density of the object

Key Takeaway
The ancients considered blackness an imaginary visual effect resulting from darkness caused by absence of light/air or the presence of water.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that blackness is not real but an illusion from blocked light. What 'darkness' in your life—a fear, a doubt, a sin—might be an illusion caused by the absence of truth? Will you let the light of knowledge penetrate that area today?
?

?
If blackness is merely the imagination of the mind, then much of what you perceive as negative may be a product of your environment. Are there beliefs you hold about yourself or others that are caused by 'absence of light' rather than reality? How will you expose them to the light of truth?

Blackness Is Real, Whiteness Is Not
An argument that blackness is real while whiteness is not, due to permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the permanence of blackness and the changeability of whiteness are used to argue that blackness is a real color while whiteness is not.

Core Idea
Permanence proves reality; changeability proves non-reality.

This brief passage presents a sharp empirical argument about the nature of colors. The author contrasts blackness, which never leaves a black body, with whiteness, which allows other colors to appear on it. This difference in permanence is taken as evidence that blackness is a real attribute while whiteness is not. The argument is simple but probing, inviting us to consider what makes something 'real' in the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without analysing. Let the argument sink in.
The Arabic text ends abruptly; the meaning is completed by the translation.
2
Identify the two comparisons
Notice how blackness and whiteness are contrasted: blackness never leaves the body, whiteness is receptive to all colors.
Receptivity here means the ability to take on other colors.
3
Understand the implied premise
Why does receptivity to other colors suggest whiteness is not real? The idea is that if a quality is real and inherent, it should persist. Change suggests accidental or imaginary status.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The argument concludes that blackness is real (a real color) and by implication whiteness is not. State this conclusion in your own words.
This is a classical kalam argument based on empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: السواد لون حقيقي فإنه لا ينسلخ عن الجسم البتة، فدل ذلك على أنه حقيقي بخلاف البياض فإن الأبيض قابل للألوان كلها والقابل لها
And it was said: Blackness is a real color, for it never leaves the body at all; that indicates that it is real, unlike whiteness, for the white is receptive to all colors, and what is receptive to them [must be devoid of them in succession].
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents a counter-argument to the view that all colors are imaginary. It argues that blackness is a real color because it never departs from a black body—a black object never becomes non-black naturally. In contrast, whiteness is not permanent, as white objects can receive other colors (they can be dyed or change color). The implication is that if whiteness were a real quality, it would not be so easily replaced. Therefore, blackness is real while whiteness is not. The argument relies on observation: black objects remain black, white objects can become colored.

Key Terms

ينسلخ
قابل للألوان
بياض
سواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument might be mistaken as supporting the reality of both colors.
Correction: It only supports blackness as real; whiteness is argued to be not real because it can be replaced.

Checkpoint
What evidence is given that blackness is a real color?
A) It is not generated like whiteness
B) It never leaves the body
C) It is visible to everyone
D) It is not imaginary

Key Takeaway
The permanence of blackness and the changeability of whiteness are used to argue that blackness is a real color while whiteness is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that permanence indicates reality. What parts of your daily life are you treating as permanent that are actually fleeting? How does this misjudgment affect your priorities in worship and relationships?
?
If blackness symbolizes what is real and unchanging in Allah's order, and whiteness symbolizes what is changeable and accidental, which 'colour' are you building your character on? What would it take for you to anchor your life more firmly in the permanent truths of the deen?

Are Black and White Real? The Ancient Debate on Color
Ancient argument: black imaginary, white real, then counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ancient arguments for black being imaginary and white being real, and the counterargument that black is a real color because it never separates from the body.

Core Idea
Black's permanence suggests it is real; white's changeability suggests it is not.

The ancients debated whether colors like black and white are real properties of objects or mere imaginings. This passage presents two opposing views: one claims black is imagined due to light conditions and water, while the other argues black is real because it never leaves the body. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two main claims: that black is imaginary, and later that black is real.
The passage begins with a brief example about whiteness.
2
Identify the evidence for black as imaginary
The ancients say blackness is imagined due to lack of air and light penetration into depths. Some say water expels air, making surfaces dark. Note the wet cloth example.
3
Examine the counterargument for black as real
The passage then shifts: black never separates from the body, while white can leave when other colors appear. This suggests black is a real, stable color.
Compare with whiteness: white is receptive to all colors, so it must be free of itself to receive others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الموضع أبيض مع كونه أبعد من حدوث البياض فيه وقد مر هذه الأمثلة في صدر الكتاب . قالوا : ( والسواد يتخيل بضد ذلك ) وهو عدم غور الهواء والضوء في عمق الجسم ( ومنهم من قال : الماء يوجب السواد) أي يوجب تخيله ( لما يخرج الهواء ) أنَّ الماء إذا وصل إلى الجسم ونفذ في أعماقه أخرج منها الهواء وليس إشفافه كإشفاف الهواء حتى ينفذ الضوء إلى السطوح فتبقى السطوح مظلمة فيتخيل أنَّ هناك سواداً. وأيضاً فإنَّ الثياب إذا ابتلت مالت إلى السواد فدل ذلك على أن الماء يوجب تخيل السواد وقيل : السواد لون حقيقي فإنه لا ينسلخ عن الجسم البتة، فدل ذلك على أنه حقيقي ( بخلاف البياض ) فإن الأبيض قابل للألوان كلها والقابل لها يكون خالياً عنها لئلا يجتمع الضدان. فإذا عرض له ما سوى البياض يجب خلوه عن البياض فقد انسلخ البياض بخلاف الأسود فإنه لا يعرض له سوى السواد حتى يجب خلوه عنه.
That place is white despite being further from the occurrence of whiteness in it, and these examples have passed in the beginning of the book. They said: 'Blackness is imagined by the opposite of that,' which is the non-penetration of air and light into the depth of the body. 'And among them is he who said: Water causes blackness,' i.e., causes its imagination, 'because it expels air,' meaning that when water reaches the body and penetrates its depths, it expels the air from it, and its transparency is not like the transparency of air so that light penetrates to the surfaces; thus the surfaces remain dark, and one imagines that there is blackness there. Also, when clothes get wet they incline to blackness, and that indicates that water causes the imagination of blackness. And it was said: Blackness is a real color, for it never separates from the body at all, and that indicates that it is real, 'unlike whiteness,' for white is receptive to all colors, and that which is receptive to them must be free of them so that two opposites do not meet. So if something other than whiteness occurs to it, it must be free of whiteness, so whiteness has separated, unlike black, for nothing other than blackness occurs to it that would necessitate its being free of it.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the ancients' view on color, specifically black and white. It begins with a statement that a place can appear white even though it is far from the actual occurrence of whiteness, referencing earlier examples. The ancients claim that blackness is imaginary, caused by the lack of penetration of air and light into the body. Some say water causes the imagination of blackness because it expels air, reducing transparency so that light does not reach the surfaces, leaving them dark. The example of wet clothes turning black supports this. Then a counterargument is presented: blackness is a real color because it never separates from the body, unlike whiteness, which separates when other colors occur to it. The reasoning is that white is receptive to all colors, so it must be free of any particular color at a given time to receive others; thus whiteness can leave the body. Black, on the other hand, never receives other colors, so it is permanent and real.

Key Terms
السواد (sawad)
البياض (bayad)
التخيل (takhayyul)
الحقيقي (haqiqi)
الانسلاخ (insilakh)
القابل (qabil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the ancients as presented, why is blackness considered a real color?
A) Because it is caused by water.
B) Because it never separates from the body.
C) Because it is imagined by the mind.
D) Because it is receptive to all colors.

Key Takeaway
The ancients debated whether black is imaginary or real, with the key distinction being that black seems to stay while white can leave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality you believe is permanent in your character—like blackness in this passage. Is it truly unchangeable, or have you simply not seen it separate? What would it take for you to admit it might be temporary?
?
You probably hold some belief that feels as solid as 'black is real.' But what if, like whiteness in this argument, your belief could be replaced by another? Are you willing to examine that possibility today?
?
The passage says white separates because it receives other colors. Is there an area of your heart where you've let a new 'color' enter, displacing what you thought was fixed? What will you do about that displacement?

intermediate
8 min
Objections to the Reality of Black and the Imagination of White
Objections and replies concerning the reality and imagination of colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the argument that black is real and white is imaginary, and how the commentator defends the original position by clarifying the meaning of 'receptive'.

Core Idea
Objections often reveal hidden assumptions about key terms.

In this passage, the author considers criticisms of the earlier argument that black is a real quality because it never leaves its subject, while white is imaginary because it can be removed. The objections challenge both points: they argue that black can also change, and that the removal of white does not prove it is unreal. The commentator then clarifies that the intended meaning is that the receiver of colors must be free of them successively, not simultaneously, which defuses the objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the full argument sink in before analyzing parts.
Notice the compressed style; each objection is introduced with 'wa-ba'na' or 'wa-'ana'.
2
Identify the three main objections
Find the three distinct objections: (1) separation of whiteness is known necessarily, (2) non-separation of black doesn't prove reality, (3) separation of whiteness doesn't prove it's imaginary.
The second objection includes two examples: youth gray hair and alchemy.
3
Understand the commentator's clarification
Read the final part where the commentator explains that the proponent meant the receiver of colors must be free of them successively, not all at once.
This resolves the objection about 'receptive'.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider how the objection misinterprets 'receptive' as requiring the receiver to be free of the quality at the moment of reception, whereas the intended meaning is successive freedom.
This is a typical example of how technical terms in kalam can be misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل انسلاخ البياض معلوم بالضرورة فما الحاجة إلى الاستدلال عليه؟ قلت : المعلوم بالضرورة انصباغ الأبيض بالألوان وهو انسلاخ البياض عنه لجواز أن يكون باستناره . واعترض عليه بأنه عدم الانسلاخ لا يدل على كونه حقيقياً إذ يجوز أن يكون سبب تخيله لازماً لبعض الأجسام على أن سواد الشباب ينسلخ بالشيب وأهل الإكسير يبيضون النحاس برصاص مكلس وزرنيخ مصعد وبأن انسلاخ البياض لا يدل على أنه تخيل لجواز أن يكون حقيقياً مفارقاً والقابل للشيء لا يجب أن يكون عنه وإلا امتنع اتصافه به فلا يكون قابلاً له. قوله : ( والقابل للشيء إلخ) حاصل هذا البحث منع كون البياض تخييلياً بمنع كبرى دليله فاللائق لترتيب البحث تقديمه على قوله : وبأن انسلاخ ،إلخ، لأنه تسليم للانسلاخ، ثم إن هذا البحث مندفع بما قررناه لأن المعلل لم يقل بأن القابل لكل شيء يجب أن يكون عاريا عنه بل القابل للألوان يجب خلوه على التعاقب .
For if it is said: The separation of whiteness is known necessarily, so what is the need for inference upon it? I say: What is known necessarily is that white is dyed with colors, and that is the separation of whiteness from it, because it is possible that this is due to its illumination. And objection was raised against it: That the non-separation does not indicate that it is real, for it is possible that the cause of its imagination is necessary for some bodies, considering that the blackness of youth separates by graying, and the people of alchemy whiten copper with calcined lead and sublimated arsenic; and that the separation of whiteness does not indicate that it is imaginary because it is possible that it is a real separate quality, and that which is receptive to something does not have to be free of it, otherwise it would be impossible for it to be described by it, so it would not be receptive to it. His saying: 'And that which is receptive to something, etc.': The gist of this investigation is the prevention of whiteness being imaginary by preventing the major premise of its proof. So it is appropriate in the order of the investigation to present it before his saying 'and that the separation, etc.' because it is an admission of the separation. Then this investigation is rebutted by what we have established, because the proponent did not say that that which is receptive to everything must be free of it, but rather that which is receptive to colors must be free of them successively.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents objections to the previous argument that black is real because it never changes and white is imaginary because it changes. The first objection is that the non-separation of black does not prove its reality; the cause of its imagined appearance might be a necessary property of some bodies. Examples are given: youthful black hair turns gray (so black does separate), and alchemists turn copper white using calcined lead and sublimated arsenic, showing that black can be removed. The second objection is that the separation of whiteness does not prove it is imaginary, because whiteness could be a real quality that leaves the body. The argument that a receiver must be free of what it receives is critiqued: if it had to be free at the moment of reception, it could never receive. The commentator then clarifies that the original proponent meant that the receiver of colors must be free of them successively, not simultaneously. Thus, the objection is defused.

Key Terms
الانسلاخ
الحقيقي
التخيلي
القابل
الإكسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The receiver of a quality must be completely free of that quality at the moment of reception.
Correction: The original argument only requires the receiver to be free of the previous color before receiving a new one, not that it must be free of all colors at once.

Checkpoint
What example is given to show that black can separate from a body?
A) Wet clothes turning black.
B) The blackness of youth turning gray.
C) The darkness of a cave.
D) The color of ink on paper.

Key Takeaway
Objections to a theological argument often stem from misinterpretations of key terms like 'receptive' or 'separation', and clarifying these terms can resolve the dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that challenges your understanding, do you first examine the key terms as this passage does, or do you quickly dismiss it? What specific term in your current beliefs might you be misunderstanding? Commit to clarifying one term this week by returning to its original source.
?
The passage shows that objections often rest on hidden assumptions—like assuming 'receptive' means always free. Consider a conviction you hold about yourself (e.g., 'I am patient' or 'I am weak'). Could it be that your conviction is based on a mistaken assumption about what that quality entails? How would you test whether it is real or merely a necessary property of your situation? What one step will you take today to examine your self-judgment with the same rigor as this lesson examines colors?
